3.44      Bid packages for LCB and ICB goods procurement have not yet been
prepared, however, no major packages are required during the initial year of the project
and commercial aspects of such procurement packages will follow well established
precedents from previous Bank projects.
3.45      Local and Foreign Consultancy Contracts (US$24.1 million). Consultants
required under the project will be hired following procedures prescribed in "Guidelines:
Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing
Agency"; August, 1981. Model documents used for inviting proposals, terms of
reference for all consultancies, and single-source contracts will be subject to prior review
for all contracts valued at US$100,000 or more awarded to firms and US$50,000 or more
to be awarded to individuals, except that all such documents as referred to in paragraphs
2.29 and 2.30, irrespective of their value, shall also be subject to prior review.
3.46      Local Training, Workshops (US$ 31.8 million).  Not a procurement
activity per se, but noted here for clarification, this category of expense relates to the
administrative costs to set up training sessions, seminars and workshops, as well as the
travel allowances and daily allowances for the tens of thousands of teachers and
administrators who will receive in service training under the auspices of the project
throughout the seven year life of the project.
3.47      IDA Review. During project supervision, all procurement under ICB as well
as the first three LCB contracts in each state for the procurement of goods and works
would be subject to prior review and approval of IDA. In addition, civil works contracts
estimated to cost US$300,000 or more and contracts for the procurement of equipment
and goods estimated to cost US$300,000 or more would be subject to prior IDA review
and approval. The review process would cover about 40% of the total contracts procured
under ICB/LCB. This relatively low percentage is considered satisfactory since the state
govemments have gained experience in procurement in previous Bank projects. Selective
post review of awarded contracts below the threshold levels would be carried out.
3.48      Procurement information will be collected and recorded as follows:
(a) prompt reporting of contract award information by the Borrower
(b) as part of comprehensive quarterly reports to IDA by the Borrower
(para. 3.23), indications of:
(i)  revised cost estimates for individual contracts and the
total project
(ii) revised timing of the procurement actions, including
advertising, bidding, contract award and completion time
for individual contracts
(iii) compliance with aggregate limits on specified methods of
procurement.



- 50 -
I. AUDITS AND ACCOUNTING
3.49      The project expenditures incurred by project states and state implementation
societies would be subject to the normal GOI and state accounting and auditing
procedures, with the added requirement that the state implementation societies would
establish and maintain separate accounts and an accounting system, to record all project
transactions and appropriate support documentation for the transactions. The accounts
of state implementation societies would be subject to audit by independent auditors,
working under terms of reference satisfactory to IDA.
3.50      Audits of Central and state accounts, the Special Account and financial
statements of the project including a separate opinion on Statement of Expenditures
(SOE) would be subject to normal GOI accounting and auditing procedures which are
considered satisfactory to IDA. Documentation supporting SOEs would be maintained
at least one year after the completion of the audit for the fiscal year in which the last
withdrawal was made. The Special Account would show all withdrawal requests
disbursed, amounts advanced and reimbursed by IDA, and balance at the end of each
accounting period. The consolidated audit reports would be submitted to the Association
not later than nine months after the end of each financial year. Audits will cover all
project expenditures until such time as the credit has been closed.



- 51 -
IV. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. BENEFITS
4.1       The proposed credit would provide substantial financial and technical support
at a critically important point in the development of primary education in India. Through
the DPEP, the GOI has made an unprecedented commitment to a sustained effort to reach
the large majority of the population who exist at the fringes of the modem economy.
Improving the human capital of the poor is the best way to help them move out of
poverty, enabling them to improve their benefits from social services, to raise their
productivity in agriculture and traditional occupations and, in the medium term, to share
in the benefits of a liberalizing economy. The proposed credit would establish the
institutional framework through which targeted assistance would reach the schools of the
poor over a phased and sustained effort over 15-20 years. The potential magnitude of
the impact of DPEP at full implementation is suggested by the number of beneficiaries
in the initial 23 IDA-assisted districts out of the total of 373 districts to be reached by
DPEP: 1,500,000 students who would complete primary schooling rather than dropping
out; 14 million students with a better chance of mastering the curriculum; and an
additional 1,400,000 primary education places.
B. RISKS
4.2       While confident that the activities that would be financed by the proposed
credit are feasible and sustainable, IDA staff and project authorities also recognize the
significant financial and implementation risks associated with the program.
FINANCING
4.3       The DPEP is aimed squarely at historically low levels of investment in
primary education, by providing investment assistance to states to, in effect, drive up
both investment and recurrent expenditures on primary education. IDA's financial
analysis indicates that the states participating in the proposed IDA credit can meet the
investment costs and sustain the recurrent expenditures of the initial set of district and
state activities (Annex 15 - Public Finances and Education Expenditures in the Six IDA
DPEP States). However, full implementation of DPEP across all targeted districts would
require unprecedented levels of investment and recurrent cost financing for all states.
For some, the revenue situation suggests that some of the growth in outlay can be met
through enhanced revenues. For most, however, sustaining the investment program
would require substantial reallocations within state budgets toward primary education.
Past experience is an uncertain guide to the willingness of the states to do so. The GOI
would partly offset state financial risks by phasing the program over two or three Plan



- 52 -
periods in order to provide an opportunity for the effects of economic adjustment and
stabilization to improve state revenues and to support reform of state budget priorities.
However, the possibility of rapid deterioration in state financial circumstances can not
be ruled out, only monitored.
4.4       At the national level, political commitment to enhanced financing for primary
education has led to substantial additional financial allocations by the Planning
Commission for DPEP. The Prime Minister has announced that outlays for education
would be increased to six percent of GDP in the Ninth Plan. However, the GOI is
experiencing difficulty in reducing the budget deficit, and this could affect allocations to
the proposed program.
4.5       Thus, for IDA and other donors, the financial risks resolve to one: the
sustainability of political commitment at the national and state levels. Initial signs are
encouraging. The enhanced Eighth Plan allocation to DPEP by the GOI is a clear sign
of increased national priority. State participation is voluntary, and those states choosing
to participate have, through their own financing studies, become aware of the medium
term investment and recurrent cost implications. In the present political climate, the
benefits of primary education of acceptable quality for the poor, and for the economy as
a whole, are increasingly well understood. In the medium term, a strong and effective
DPEP could reinforce political commitment.
4.6       Close monitoring of the indicators of political commitment, notably annual
budget allocations, would be a priority for IDA and other donors. In this respect, it is
worth noting that the EC would release approximately US$90 million to the program in
four equal tranches tied to maintenance of FY91/92 budgets for elementary education in
real terms in annual GOI budgets for the DOE. Subsequent credits from IDA would be
dependent on satisfactory financing and implementation progress in the first credit.
These macro-incentives could provide continuing support for existing high levels of
political commitment to primary education and to the DPEP.
4.7       IDA staff conclude, on balance, that the present high level of financial and
political commitment, supported by close monitoring and macro-incentives, justify the
financial risks associated with the proposed credit.
IMPLEMENTATION
4.8       Managing a program of the scope and complexity of DPEP will not be easy.
The risk is further raised by the necessary move away from relatively easily-administered
central programs based on norms to a program that seeks, in principle, to provide
integrated development assistance tied to local conditions. To achieve the necessary level
of management capacity, the DPEP would need to create new management structures at
national, state and district levels.
4.9       There is a thus a risk that implementation would be uneven across the states
in the initial stages of activity. This could lead to slow disbursements in early years.



- 53 -
There is also risk attached to the proposed efforts to mobilize national and state expertise
to support and, over time, improve the quality of implementation and interventions at the
district level.
4.10      These risks are balanced in part by the way the proposed project has been
developed, in part by elements of program design and in part by actions taken by the
GOI and states. However, residual uncertainties regarding implementation arrangements
would remain, as is the case for most innovative projects in constrained financial
circumstances.
4.11      The project preparation process gives some assurances that these management
structures can function as intended. The pace of preparation for the proposed project has
been swift. The accomplishments of the GOI, states and districts in project preparation
have been substantial, indicating significant political commitment and ownership. A high
degree of popular participation has been achieved.
4.12      A number of elements of the program design combine to strengthen risk
management. First, in designing the DPEP the GOI has drawn explicitly on lessons of
experience from externally-assisted state primary education projects. This experience has
demonstrated the importance of strong institutional capacity, a key objective of DPEP.
Second, the proposed DPEP Bureau would be well staffed for supervision and appraisal
and would operate through an annual management control mechanism, with full
participation of donors. Third, there is an explicit provision for a start-up year focused
on a limited set of key activities, including institutional development.
4.13      Substantial actions designed to facilitate effective startup would be taken by
the time the proposed credit would be presented to the Bank's Board. At the national
level, the entire project development team would be appointed to key program
management positions in the DPEP Bureau. All key officials and state and district levels
would be appointed, with substantial continuity with project preparation teams. Program
rules and regulations and finance and accounting systems would be in place, supported
by an implementation manual. Contracts for key services would have been pre-approved
by IDA and ready to sign. Detailed first year work programs and budgets would have
been approved by program authorities. Substantial funds would have been released by the
GOI to state implementation societies and the program would have been launched.
4.14      The program would create incentives for good performance. In combination
with performance measurement, the management mechanism would create incentives for
quality implementation by states and districts. Program guidelines would permit non-
performing districts of states to be dropped from the program.  By putting financial
incentives in place to support key national and state technical support programs, the
DPEP would introduce a new approach to mobilizing expertise in the social sectors.
Extensive popular participation has generated substantial interest in the benefits of the
program in blocks and communities. If these interests are met through effective
implementation, growing demand from beneficiaries should provide another incentive
for implementation at the lowest levels of the program.



- 54 -
4.15      International donors would mobilize considerable technical resources to
support DPEP. IDA and the EC would strengthen their technical expertise in resident
missions to support implementation. IDA human resources staff based in the New Delhi
Mission would provide continuous technical and managerial support; the Procurement and
Disbursement Unit in the Mission would provide technical assistance in these key areas.
The EC is posting a senior human resources expert to New Delhi to manage a US$5
million technical assistance fund for DPEP. UNESCO has also expanded its New Delhi
staff to include an expert in educational statistics and monitoring to support DPEP.
UNICEF has committed its extensive local expert staff resources to project preparation
and would continue to assist with implementation.
4.16      Finally, periodic beneficiary assessments and a substantial program of
evaluation and research should provide a well-substantiated flow of academic and public
information on program performance, strengthening the incentive structure for national
and state program management.
4.17      However, residual risks remain for financial flows and accountability,
relationships between state governments and implementation societies, and relationships
between the program and newly elected local governments under the Panchayat Raj
legislation.
4.18      Financial Flows. DPEP would create a new system of financial advances
to state implementation societies. The system has been created to overcome diversion
of development assistance by state finance departments and thus to improve
implementation and disbursements. New accounting and auditing arrangements would
be established. There is a risk that despite careful design and appraisal, new systems
would not function as intended. This risk, however, must be balanced by the problems
inherent in present systems.
4.19      Relationships between State Govermnents and Societies. While previous
experience with state implementation societies is encouraging, the proposed scale of
operations through societies is unprecedented.  The GOI has designed societies to
function as project implementation structures for state govemments, with direct state
control through the composition of goveming councils and executive committees, and
through annual control (with the GOI) of funding for society activities. During appraisal,
IDA voiced concems regarding the nature of the legal relationships between societies and
state govemments. The GOI's responses to these concerns have been found to provide
a satisfactory basis to proceed while recognizing that uncertainties would persist for the
first few years of operations of the proposed project.
4.20      Relationships with Local Governnents. The establishment of locally elected
govemments (Panchayat Raj bodies) in all states draws on long national commitments to
local democracy. However, with implementing legislation only recently passed in all
states, there is considerable uncertainty regarding the possible impact of local governance
on primary education. The extent to which district govemments would control financial



- 55 -
allocations and teacher appointments, for example, could have major and varying
implications for the implementation of DPEP, both positive and negative.
4.21      The capacity of the proposed program to monitor and manage these risks
would be strengthened through the proposed credit. Both project authorities and IDA
recognize these residual risks, and would agree to monitor and discuss developments
annually. It has been agreed that an independent management review of the functioning
of societies, including financial mechanisms, would be conducted in the third year of the
project. DPEP would carry out a continuing program of studies to monitor the impact
of the Panchayat Raj legislation, and discuss these studies with IDA.



- 56 -
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED
5.1       At negotiations, the GOI provided assurances that:
(a)  terms of reference and tender documents for technical assistance
programs for a) pedagogical improvement, b) educational planning and
management and c) ST education development would be provided for
IDA review by January 15, 1995 (paragraph 2.30);
(b)  IDA concurrence would be sought for preparation of any state or
district sub-projects additional to the initial 23 proposed for the credit,
(with full appraisal by DPEP and IDA appraisal of a sample of
proposed state/district expansion sub-projects) (paragraph 2.37);
(c)  the project would be implemented in accordance with DPEP
Guidelines, and proposed changes in DPEP Guidelines would be
discussed with IDA (paragraph 2.47);
(d)  DIETs would be established, fully staffed and functioning in all
project districts by January 31, 1996 (paragraph 2.59);
(e)  GOI would ensure that assessment studies are carried out in all project
districts in the third and sixth year of the project using methods
satisfactory to IDA (paragraph 3.23);
(f)  GOI would conduct a mid-term review of project implementation and
impact, including disbursements, improvements in dropout rates,
learning achievement scores, and enrollments, with IDA by June 30,
1998 (paragraph 3.23);
(g)  an independent management review  of the functioning of state
implementation societies would be conducted under terms of reference
satisfactory to IDA by December 1, 1997 (paragraph 3.24).



- 57 -
5.2        At negotiations, the project states provided assurances that:
(a)  revised strategies for each state and district for improving primary
education for girls and ST students based on discussion of project
studies, together with implementation plans and budgets for the second
year of the project, would be presented for IDA review by January
15, 1995 (paragraph 2.75);
(b)  states shall allocate Project resources for tribal students at least in
proportion to the share of the tribal population in the district
population and shall provide for IDA review all proposals for the
establishment of ST residential schools, complete with school maps
and evidence of demand for such schools on the part of isolated
communities, prior to the authorization of construction (paragraph
2.78);
5.3.       There would be no conditions of effectiveness.
5.4.       Subject to the above assurances, the proposed project constitutes a suitable
basis for an IDA Credit of SDR 180 million (US$260.3 million equivalent) to India at
standard IDA terms with 35 years maturity.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Project Preparation Process
1.           The District Primary Edcucationi Program  (DPEP) is a national financial
intermediary and technical support organization created to manage a rapidly expandling
investment program  for primary education.  The program  would serve as the nmain
vehicle for utilizing external assistance to primary education.
2.            Preparation of DPEP proposals for external financing required that the
DOE address three substantial tasks in parallel.  First, an analytical basis for the
development and refinemiient of intervenltionl strategies and to establish baselines for
assessment of progress would need to be created. Second, project development in a large
federal system would require decentralizationi of planning in order to strengthen popular
participation, local ownerslhip and( eventLual ii iplementation. Third, a nationlal capacity
for appraisal, financing and supervisioni of state and district suLb-projects would need to
be created at the same time that state andl district proposals woulcl be developed.
3.            Preparation Strategy. The strategy of the DOE has been to use the process
of completing the three preparation tasks as an opportunity to organize and develop the
institutional capacity that would be needed for program  implemnenitationi and futLure
expansion. Formal training, national techlical assistanice and learninig by doing have
been the elements of the capacity bulilding strategy for each task.
Studies Program
4.            The program  of project preparation  studies is sumlmliiarize(d in Annex 4.
In addition to providing the analytical basis needed for initial and evolving intervention
designs, the studies program provided a structuLre for capacity building at national, state
and district levels.
5.            The national research teamls from] the National CoullCil for Educational
Research and Training (NCERT) and the National Institute of Educatiolnal Planning and
Administration (NIEPA) organized and trained research teams in states and districts, and
some of the data analysis was carried out in state institLutions.  The formal training
provided and extensive experience in the mianiagemiienit and supervision of large scale rLural
educational research has established an initial level of capacity in states and some
districts. In all, inore than 250 researchers were trained in the process. This capacity
would be drawn upon to conduct follow-tip assessments to mieasuLre project impact in the
third and sixth years of the project as well as to carry out evaluation studies of particular
DPEP interventions.

Decentralized Planning
6.           Decentralization of education planning and management to districts is a
central objective of the Revised National Policy of Education (1992). The policy is
further strengthened by the 73rd and 74th Amen(dliments to the Constitutioll of India,
which provide for increased authority and responsibility for primary education, amzong
other programs, to district governments. The revised national education policy also calls
for popular participation in the development and imnplementation of primary education.
7.           Capacity Building. The DPEP Guidelinles issued by the Departmenit of
Education require that planning be done at district level with substantial popular
participation. While district plaiiilng for primary education hlad been done on a limiited
pilot basis in the past, preparation for proposed DPEP investments would be the first
time that it would be attempted on a large scale.  To strengthen district planiling
capacity, faculty from NIEPA and NCERT were appointed as chief consuiltanits to each
participating state. These individuals participated in the lauiilch of planning exercises at
state and district levels, and provided continIlilIg independent advice and technical
assistance during the process.
8.           In addition, NIEPA organized a series of traininlg programs on educational
planning for state and district planning teams, and conducted workshops on key elemelits
of plan development (costing, civil works planning, work planinlilg) at different stages
during plan development at which emerging plans were reviewed and revised. NCERT
provided similar workshop support for in-service teacher training, textbook publication,
and strategy development for girls education.
9.           Popular Participation. State and district planning teams were asked by
DOE/DPEP to keep track of mzeetinigs heldl for the purposes of consultations with
officials, commuinity leaders, teachers, NGOs and miemilbers of comimluniities. The scale
of the consultation process is suggested by Annex 3 Table 1, which has been compiled
from state and district reports and participant lists and counts. The data indicate that
well in excess of 50,000 persons participated in planining discussions at block, village and
school levels. Meetings at the district and state levels often involved the same persons;
yet the numbers of meetings and participants are still impressive.
10.          By and large, district level irleetings involved educationi officials, members
of legislature, teachers, and adminiistrators of related programs, such as ICDS. In middle
and later stages, these meetinigs often focuse(d oin reviews of evolving plan docuinents,
and on assignmenits for continiuing plan developmient.

Appraisal and Supervision Capacity
17.          The project development team in the GOI Department of Education began
preparation of the DPEP programii with two years of experience with thle preparation an(l
implementation of externally-finaliced state basic eduication projects in Andhra Pradesh
(ODA), Bihar (UNICEF), Rajasthan (SIDA) ancl Uttar Pradesh (IDA). This experience
had convinced the DOE that rather than rely on the disparate appraisal and supervision
procedures of different external agencies, nationial capacity to carry olut these fuiilctions
should be developed if India were to become fully self-reliant in maniaging her rapidly
expanding primary education investmienit programl.
18.          Full establishment of the DPEP maniagemilent structure would have to await
the approval of anticipated financing support from the European Coinmunlity and IDA.
In the interim, a project development team of three experienced deputy secretaries led
full time by a joint secretary was established in the DOE. Flinds were provided by the
DOE to be passed to the states for project developmenit costs. The National Core Team
of experts from NCERT and NIEPA was establishecl and finaniced to provide technical
assistance to states and districts.
19.          BLuilding Staff Capacity. Two strategies were employed to build staff and
consultant capacity for appraisal of sub-projects. First, DOE project development team
members participated fully in IDA preparation, pre-appraisal and appraisal missions.
By appraisal, the project team conducted its own formal appraisal of proposals in
collaboration with the IDA team.
20.          Second, the DOE project team] was itself carrying otit the project cycle for
the proposed grant from the European Comilmuillity, withi preparation, pre-appraisal and
appraisal missions scheduiled to come after IDA had completed each stage of project
processing. The appraisal process for the European ComIlLullity grant was carriedl ouLt
largely in Madhya Pradesh.  DOE involved mem)bers of the National Core Team,
external consultants financed by EC, as well as several consuiltants who were also
participating in the IDA DPEP appraisal. In later stages of the this appraisal process,
state and district officials from  the states developing proposals for IDA  were also
involved in order to begin the development of a roster of experienced consultants from
different parts of the couLntry.

21.           Both series of appraisal activities were expressly conceived of as on-the-job
training experiences for project development team memiibers and national consultants.
More than thirty national staff and consultants participated over a twelve mlonitlh period.
22.           Refining Criteria and Procedures. The DPEP Guidelines issued by the
DOE in April, 1993 provided substantial early guidanice to districts and states for the
preparation of sub-project proposals. At the same time, as proposals were reviewed at
different stages, the need for addlitional criteria and guidelinles emerged. These were
initially codified for IDA's pre-appraisal mission; they were formially structured and
issued prior to IDA's final appraisal.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Project Preparation Studies
1.       Six project studies were completed in project states and districts. Three are
primary education beneficiary assessments: (a) baseline assessments of learning
achievement, (b) studies of factors affecting enrollment and dropout for girl students, and
(c) studies of the educational context and needs of Scheduled Tribe (ST) students. Three
more studies focus on key elements of DPEP program design: (a) teacher education,
training, recruitment and motivation; (b)  textbook preparation, publication and
distribution; and (c) state education financing.
2.       The studies were carried out by teams led by scholars from the National
Council for Educational Research and Training (NCERT) and the National Institute for
Educational Planning and Administration (NIEPA). Study teams were drawn from
research and training institutions in participating districts and states. Training in research
and analysis methodology was provided by the study team coordinators, both formally
and through supervision of data collection and analysis, and report writing. IDA and
UNICEF assisted in the preparation of terms of reference for the studies, and provided
the financing required. IDA financing came from a grant from Policy and Human
Resources Development Fund of the Government of Japan.
A. BENEFICIARY ASSESSMENTS
Baseline Assessments of Learning Achievement
3.       The objectives of these studies were: (a) to assess the achievement level of
students who were nearing the end of the primary cycle in the districts under the DPEP
program and to investigate differences in achievement of the different groups of students
(boys/girls, SC/ST students and rural and urban students); (b) to estimate the level of
learning of simple skills of literacy and numeracy achieved at the end of class 1 and of
students who had dropped out after 3 or 4 years of schooling, and (c) to collect data on
relevant pupil background and school factors that could explain differences in the
learning achievement of pupils at the end of the primary cycle.
4.       Design and methodology. Multi-stage random sampling was used to select
rural and urban blocks, schools and students. Six instruments were administered in each
school in the sample: (a) a standardized achievement test in reading comprehension for



- 78 -
Annex 4
Page 2
grade 4 or 51'; (b) a standardized achievement test in mathematics for grades 4 or 5; (c)
a test of basic competencies in reading and arithmetic expected by end of grade 1; (d)
a simple literacy test for dropouts; (e) an interview schedule for students at the end of
the primary cycle and for dropouts; (f) an interview schedule for teachers; and (g) a
schedule for recording data from school records. All instruments were administered by
interviewers trained in the classroom and in the field.
5.         Sample. The total sample for the six states and 23 districts being proposed to
IDAV' consisted of 850 schools, 2712 teachers, 14,341 pupils in the last year of primary
school, 13,021 pupils in grade 2, and 1099 children who had dropped out of school.
6.         Selected findings. Selected findings from  the initial tabulation of data are:3'
Average  achievement  levels  in  reading  comprehension  and
mathematics for grades 4/5 are extremely low in all project
districts;4'
*          Average achievement levels for class 2 students and dropouts are
also very low;
Differences in achievement between girls and boys and SC/ST
students and others are small, without a consistent pattern across
districts and states;
The largest share of dropout occurs in grades 1 and 2;
*          On average, 90 percent of students have all required textbooks;
More than 90 percent of schools have blackboards;
*          In some states, schools are not in session for the prescribed number
of days;
The primary cycle is four or five years long, depending on states. Competencies tested are those expected by the end of
the next to last year of schooling (grades 3 or 4).
2 Identical studies were carried out in 24 districts in two additional states being considered for external financial support
from other sources.
These are provided to give a sense of the study results; initial cross tabulations run to 110 tables per district.
See Annex S for test scores.



- 79 -
Annex 4
Page 3
*        With the exception of Maharashtra, teaching is highly didactic and
not suitable for the prevalent multi-grade teaching situations;
*        There is considerable inter-state variation in pupils' ability to
understand the language of the teacher.
7.       The finding of the assessments suggest that low levels of learning are do more
to weak classroom processes and low quality of inputs (as opposed to the availability of
basic inputs). They support the general DPEP intervention strategies of improving the
quality of teaching and learning materials, with an emphasis on the early primary grades,
and of mobilizing community involvement in schools to reduce dropout.
Studies of Girls' Education
8.       Gender studies were undertaken in project districts to provide inputs into the
formulation of plans for girls' education and women's empowerment. The study process
was also used to mobilize and organize support for improvements in primary education
for girls.
9.       Design and methodologv. There are two parts to each study: (a) a review of
existing state and district policies and interventions for girls' education, including a
review of curricula and textbooks for gender bias; and (b) a largely qualitative field study
conducted through semi-structured interviews and group discussions with district and
block officials, teachers and community leaders, parents, girl students, girl dropouts and
girls who had never enrolled in schools. The field study focused on perceptions of
causes of continuing girls in school, of non-enrollment, of dropout, and of gender
equality in communities.
10.      Each study report also contains strategies and action plans based on the
findings of the research.
11.      Sample.  One of the three blocks selected at random  for the baseline
assessments of learning achievement was selected in each district. Within this block,
studies were conducted in eight villages.  For each block, interviews were completed
with 30-40 teachers, 50-60 girl students, 20-30 dropouts and students never enrolled and
20-25 school and community officials and administrators. Group discussions involved
on average 250 households per block. In total, approximately 15,000 parents and
neighbors, 650 teachers, 1100 students, 600 dropouts and never enrolled students and
500 administrators and officials participated in the studies.
12.      Selected findings.  The studies documented a wide variety of current
interventions intended to improve female enrollment and attendance, principally in the
form of incentives such as free books, uniforms and attendance allowances. Curriculum



- 80 -
Annex 4
Page 4
and textbook analyses indicated substantial gender bias: for example, grade 1 textbooks
in Assam have no illustrations of women and girls; in the class 2 mathematics textbook,
only boys names were used in examples and problems. DPEP support for curriculum
and textbook review and improvement would include preparation of gender fair textbook
and learning materials for the schools.
13.      Selected initial field study findings include:
*       low levels of women's participation in panchayats and other
community organizations;
with variation across districts, economic reasons (inability to pay
fees, girl needed for household work) were cited by parents as
causes of non-enrollment and dropout;
teachers are likely to cite low levels of parental education and
motivation as causes of non-enrollment and dropout;
discussions with parents reveal that teacher absenteeism  and
engagement of teachers in other duties make parents reluctant to
send girls to school;
*        interviews with girl students indicate that outright discrimination or
harassment by teachers is rarely cited as a reason for not attending
school.
14.      One of initial patterns emerging from these studies is that even within small
villages significant differences in perceptions regarding issues in girls education exist
among parents, girls and teachers. The DPEP strategy to establish or strengthen village
education committees, with at least one-third of the members being female, is intended
to help improve communication between teachers and parents, and parental interest in
helping to ensure that schools function properly.
Studies of ST Education
15.      Studies of the availability and use of educational services by ST children, and
of the attitudes of parents, teachers, community leaders and community members were
conducted by state and districts investigation teams trained and supervised by NCERT.
The study was carried out in five states proposed for IDA financing (the sixth, Haryana,
has no ST students).  At appraisal, data were available for Assam, Karnataka,
Maharashtra and Tamil Nadu. The Kerala study would be completed by August, 1994.



- 81 -
Annex 4
Page 5
16.      Design and methodology. The study had two components in each state. The
first was a status report that summarizes secondary information on the demographics of
tribal peoples, the tribal development plan programs, and available educational schemes
and programs. The second was a field study carried out in blocks with highest ST
population in 2 of the baseline achievement study sample districts in four states, and one
baseline district in Maharashtra, for a total of 5 districts. Eight questionnaires were
administered by trained investigators to students, parents, community leaders, teachers,
and nonformal education instructors. Group discussions were also conducted in each
community and the results recorded in investigators' diaries.
17.      Sample. In nine project districts the blocks with the highest proportion of ST
population were selected for the field study. In these blocks, the ST population averages
25 percent of the total (the national average is 12 percent), with a range of 15 to 60
percent. There is one comparatively low density block (Kasargode in Kerala), where 04
percent of the total population is ST. Ten villages were selected within each block;
within each village, investigators conducted interviews with 12 students, 5-15 teachers
(depending on the number of schools), 10 parents, 2 male and 3 female community
members. Taking into account some variation in numbers across villages, the total
sample for the four completed studies consists of 70 villages, 840 students, 700 teachers
and nonformal education instructors, 700 parents, and 350 community members. For a
given block, the sample consisted of 120 students, 50-150 teachers, 100 parents, and 50
community members. Administrators, officials and community leaders participated in
group discussions.
18.      Selected findings. Selected findings from the draft synthesis report of state
studies include:
*        In all states, virtually all ST children have access to a primary
school in the village or within 1 km walking distance from the
village;
*        Hostels (Ashrams) for ST students were most frequently found in
villages in Maharashtra (90 percent) and Karnataka (50 percent);
availability in the remaining 2 states was 5-15 percent.
*        Relatively few of these Ashrams provide education on premise
(Assam - 0 percent; Karnataka - 15 percent; Tamil Nadu 30
percent. Maharashtra is an exception, with 60 percent of Ashrams
providing education on premise;
*        The proportion of villages with NGOs providing education services
varies (Maharashtra - 0 percent; Kamataka - 5 percent; Tamil Nadu
- 20 percent; Assam 40 percent).



- 82 -
Annex 4
Page 6
*        The proportion of females among teachers range from 05 percent
(Maharashtra) to 40 percent (Assam);
The percentage of teachers residing in or near the village ranges
from 50 percent (Maharashtra) to 75 percent (Assam and Tamil
Nadu);
The percentage of teachers citing problems in teaching ST children
ranged from 36 percent in Tamil Nadu to more than 70 percent in
Assam and Maharashtra); in Karnataka and Maharashtra, the most
frequently cited problem is that of language; in Assam and Tamil
Nadu, teachers most frequently cite the students "poor grasping
power. "
For girl ST students, the problem most frequently cited by teachers
is household work;
*        Three quarters or more of children have their textbooks, and most
express positive opinions of them;
*        As reported by parents, there is wide variation in the use of
dominant regional languages in the home (Tamil Nadu- 100 percent;
Karnataka - 79 percent; Assam - 54 percent; Maharashtra - 30
percent);
*        The proportion of teachers using ST languages in the classroom
varies (Tail Nadu - 4 percent; Karnataka - 25 percent; Assam - 30
percent; Maharashtra - 36 percent);
The proportion of parents preferring the use of ST languages in the
classroom ranged from less than 5 percent in Tamil Nadu to 12 -
15 percent in Assam, Maharashtra and Karnataka.
19.      These selected findings suggest that most ST students have access to schools,
but that family requirements for household labor reduce participation, especially for girls.
Language problems emerge as obstacles to effective classroom process in some states,
but not in others. In combination with the comparatively high proportion of teachers
residing outside of the school community, language problems suggest that lack of
communication in the classroom is an important problem in locations other than Tamil
Nadu.   However, parents generally prefer to have children taught in the regional
language. While further analysis is needed, the data suggest that DPEP could support
general strategies aimed at improving regional language skills of children in early



- 83 -
Annex 4
Page 7
primary grades. The variation across the blocks studies also suggests that some strategies
would need to be specific to the district and indeed to blocks. The initial strategies to
be supported by DPEP do vary across districts (see Annex 9: Education of Scheduled
Castes and Scheduled Tribes).
B. PROGRAM DESIGN STUDIES
20.      Previous studies conducted by NCERT and NIEPA, as well as studies
sponsored by IDA in Tamil Nadu and Uttar Pradesh had confirmed general understanding
that improvements in the quality of teaching and textbooks would need to be supported
by DPEP. In addition, DPEP Guidelines require that DPEP funding be additional to
real growth in state budgets for elementary education, and that recurrent costs of DPEP
supported activities would be sustainable by states on non-plan budgets at the end of the
project. To inform DPEP program design, studies were therefore carried out in all six
project states on teacher education, training and recruitment policies as well as teacher
motivation and problems; on textbook preparation and publication; and on state education
finances. These studies were carried out by state teams under the supervision of scholars
at NCERT and NIEPA according to terms of reference agreed reviewed by IDA.
Teacher Study
21.      NCERT carried out an analysis of policies relating to teacher education,
training and recruitment policies supplemented by a field study of a sample of teachers
in DPEP districts to gather information on teacher motivation and problems faced by
teachers.
22.      Design and methodologv. Documentation of state policy (both in theory and
practice) was done by analyzing government orders and procedural guidelines as well as
by interviewing members of the SCERT faculty, District and Block Education Officers.
In order to analyze the district-level facilities, the DIET or the appropriate teacher
training institute was visited. In order to study teacher in-service training needs and
perceptions, a sample of teachers were interviewed.  Guidelines were developed to
interview educational administrators regarding the state policies towards teachers. An
interview schedule administered to the faculty of the SCERT was used to collect
information regarding the manner of in-service training needs assessment procedure and
its role in the training of teachers. An interview schedule for District/Block Education
Officers was used to assess the academic support provided by them to teachers and their
views regarding the in-service training needs of primary school teachers. A training
institution information schedule was developed to assess the facilities of the DIET or
other teacher training institutions. An interview schedule was administered to the sample
teachers.



- 84 -
Annex 4
Page 8
23.      The instruments were pre-tested on a small sample of teachers and at one
DIET. The research team developed a simple training-cum-field manual which was used
by the field team. Field work was carried out by a team of 3 field investigators and
senior consultants were appointed to collect data from the state authorities and teacher
training institutes.
24.      Sample. For the interviews with teachers, two project districts in each of the
states were selected purposively; the criteria for selection were the existence of a DIET
and whether the district was a tribal district. One block in each district and one school
complex within each district were selected randomly. One urban area (the district
headquarters) was also studied. In states which do not have the system of school
complexes, primary schools in the catchment area of a secondary school were treated as
a cluster of schools. In each district, the sample size was restricted to 100 teachers,
distributed across rural and urban areas using the proportion of rural and urban
population. Where the number of teachers was fewer than 100, teachers from the
adjacent school complex were included in the sample. In all, 1,200 teachers were
interviewed in 12 districts of the 6 DPEP states.
25.      Selected findings. Selected findings from the study include:
*        Several states have upgraded the minimum academic qualifications
required by teachers to 12 years (Haryana, Maharashtra and Tamil
Nadu); however, the existing stock of teachers includes a number
of teachers with only 10 and in some cases just 8 years of
education. About 15 percent of teachers in Assam, 10 percent on
Maharashtra and 03 percent in Kerala (mostly Urdu/Arabic
teachers) have less than 10 years of schooling.
*        With the exception of Assam, all states require primary school
teachers to obtain a diploma in teacher training. Between 95-99
percent of teachers in the system in these states are already trained.
In Assam, the proportion of untrained teachers is as high as 42
percent (35,000).
*        In general, there is an excess supply of teachers in all states except
in Assam, where supply falls short of demand and in Haryana,
where demand and supply are more or less in equilibrium.
*        Recruitment procedures vary from  state to state. State-wide
recruitment by a central authority is carried out in Assam, Haryana,
Kerala and Tamil Nadu but teachers are normally appointed within
their own districts. In Kamataka and Maharashtra, recruitment is
carried out at the district level.



- 85 -
Annex 4
Page 9
*        Only Haryana, a relatively small state which is well connected by
public transport, offers an additional allowance for posting in rural
and unserved areas. Karnataka, Kerala and Tamil Nadu offer a hill
area allowance but it is unclear whether this is sufficient to
compensate teachers for the additional costs of moving to such
areas. As a result, many vacant posts in remote rural areas and hilly
tracts remain unfilled for long periods of times.
*        Compulsory transfer of teachers is practiced in Maharashtra,
Karnataka and Haryana after every 3-5 years, following the general
rules of the civil service. This policy may lead to teacher
dissatisfaction and impose an additional economic burden on them
for which they are not compensated. In the other states, teachers are
transferred either on request or on administrative grounds alone.
*        In Maharashtra, the official policy calls for teachers to be posted at
least 25 kilometers away from their residence. However, since there
are usually no residential facilities in rural areas, especially for
female teachers, many teachers prefer to stay in nearby towns and
commute long distances every day.
*        In all the states, teachers in government and aided schools are given
regular appointments on salary scales that are linked to the civil
service pay structure. Assam alone allows for teachers to be
appointed on an ad hoc basis on a consolidated salary (which
amounts to about one-half the salary of regular teachers) until they
secure their training.
*        Salary increments are related solely to seniority. No state provides
for rewarding effective teachers, although good teachers are given
public recognition through teachers' awards.
*        Despite the recent revisions in the pre-service teacher education
curriculum, theory accounts for 40-60 percent of prescribed course
time, the remaining being allotted to practice teaching The course
content does not reflect the teaching of practical skills such as
multi-grade teaching or the teaching of reading, nor is it
meaningfully related to the Minimum Levels of Learning
competency-based approach to curriculum transaction.
*        In most states, only about 10 percent of the teachers receive in-
service training annually.



- 86 -
Annex 4
Page 10
*        Facilities for in-service training programs are usually restricted to
the district headquarters. Only Karnataka and Maharashtra offer
training programs that are delivered at the block level, while there
is no systematic training program at the school complex level.
*        In Kerala, the proportion of female teachers exceeds that of male
teachers, while in Haryana and Tamil Nadu, the proportions are
roughly equal. In Assam females comprise about 23-25 percent of
the teaching force.
*        Most in-service courses lasted between 8 to 15 days.  Subject
matter training and general methods of teaching dominated the
content of these programs. In some districts, between 25-30 percent
of teachers who had received in-service training said they could not
change their classroom practices as a result of the training due to
non-availability of the required material, heavy work load and
irrelevance of training. There was no training in multi-grade
teaching, the preparation and use of teaching aids.
*        An overwhelming percentage of teachers (usually over 90 percent)
reported the need for in-service training.
*        In order to improve the efficacy of training programs, the majority
of teachers listed consultation with them regarding their training
needs, and providing support to them in implementing new
ideas/innovations acquired during training programs, the availability
of competent resource persons as the most important factors.
*        On the whole, teachers in Karnataka, Kerala, Maharashtra and
Tamil Nadu felt that the social status of teachers has improved over
the last decade. In Assam and Haryana, half or more of the teachers
felt that their status had declined.
*        Most teachers also felt that their economic status was 'reasonable'
although most said that they could not meet all the needs of their
family with their salary income alone. The majority of teachers
owned a house (though some had inherited the house and had not
acquired through their own earnings) and a bicycle.
*        Less than 20 percent of teachers had been promoted sometime
during their career and the majority said that they had no chance of
being promoted.



- 87 -
Annex 4
Page I 1
*        Female teachers in remote districts cited the difficulty of getting
residential accommodation as one of their main problems. The lack
of separate toilets was also another commonly highlighted problem.
*        A high proportion of teachers also reported that the apathy of
parents towards their children's education as one of their problems.
Relatively few teachers reported academic guidance and support
from district and block education officers.
26.      DPEP would initially focus on improving in-service teacher training is a major
component of DPEP, including development of training infrastructure to the block and
school complex level. The study suggests that, in addition, that states would have to
undertake a comprehensive review of policies towards pre-service education, admission
criteria, recruitment and remuneration procedures and introducing methods for
systematically assessing training needs. While teachers are apparently not dissatisfied
with their economic or social status, female teachers and teachers in remote areas do face
particular problems which must be addressed. These issues, however, would take some
time to resolve. DPEP would support the continuing discussion of policy options.
Textbooks
27.      Three separate studies were undertaken to obtain comparative information on
textbook provision from the states involved and likely to become involved in DPEP. With
the help of funding from UNICEF, the staff of National Council for Education Research
and Training (NCERT) and the IDA textbook specialist developed the terms of reference
and instruments for the studies.  Study teams in each project state participated in
implementation of the reviews.
28.      Design and methodologv. Two assessments were completed. The first was
a Study on the Designing, Production and Distribution of Instructional Materials and
NFE Materials. It contained a comprehensive series of questions on a wide range of
aspects of textbook development, production and distribution. These included details of
the organizational structure in the states, the number of titles produced and the number
of languages involved, the physical characteristics of the books, sources and costs of
manuscript development and review, sources and costs of illustration and design,
processes, sources and costs of production, book pricing formulae, discounts, distribution
methods, finance - particularly relating to subsidy and free textbook provision - and
distribution in each state.  The second was a Study of Paper and Infrastructure for
Printing. This covered sources of paper, printing and binding in greater detail and
sought further information on distribution outlets. Data gathering instruments for both
studies were completed by research teams under the supervision of NCERT staff.



- 88 -
Annex 4
Page 12
29.      Additionally, all states were asked to submit copies of their full range of
primary textbooks. These were reviewed by a team of experts and given ratings for the
quality of their design and production.
30.      Selected findings. Selected findings from the two studies include:
*        In many states the relationships between the various
entities that develop curriculum, develop textbooks,
publish textbooks and distribute textbooks are imprecise
and there is no effective overall management structure.
This may result in poor textbook quality (content,
presentation and production), delays and inefficiency in
textbook supply and excessive cost in textbook provision
as a whole.
*        In  some  States the  flow  of funds for textbook
manufacture and supply is slow and Textbook
Corporations or their equivalent are obliged to borrow
working capital at high rates of interest.
*        Almost all States keep textbook prices relatively low by
economizing on the quality of paper, printing and
binding. Some States also subsidize textbook production
and distribution. The formulae used to arrive at book
selling prices rarely take into account all the costs
incurred in development, manufacture and distribution.
*        There is a trend to supplying free primary textbooks not
only to SCs and STs but also to all girls and,
increasingly frequently, to all children.
*        Although textbook publishing is a specialized industry
and the States are large scale publishers, the skill levels
of those employed by the States are not high and most
States have insufficient or no specialist staff.
*        Not all primary  school textbooks are written and
reviewed by practicing primary school teachers. Virtually
no primary textbooks are trialled before their
introduction throughout a State.
*        Authors,  reviewers  and  illustrators  are  poorly
remunerated.



- 89 -
Annex 4
Page 13
There is wide variation from State to State in the number
of pages considered appropriate for a specific subject at
a specific Class level.
The quality of typesetting, illustration, page layout, book
design and production of the majority of the States'
primary textbooks received ratings of 'average' or
'poor'.
*        Textbooks are expected to last no longer than one year.
As yet, no State plans to raise textbook quality and
encourage book re-use.
*        With  the  exception  of some  titles published  by
Maharashtra and Assam all primary textbooks are wire
stitched. For lower Classes two or four color printing is
used by most States.
.States that are obliged to use State owned printing
resources generally have greater difficulty in containing
costs and in making books available at the beginning of
the school year than States that are free to use whichever
printers they choose.
*        States are not as successful in providing textbooks for
children speaking minority languages as they are in
providing them for children speaking the principal
language or languages of the State.
*        The methods used by states for arriving at the number of
textbooks to print are haphazard and frequently lead to
unacceptable shortages or surpluses.
*        Retailing and distribution are handled by the States in a
variety of different ways. Some States use only private
sector resources, others use only public sector. Those
States that use a combination of private and public sector
generally do better in reaching all parts of the state with
new textbooks in time for the start of the school year.
31.      These issues have been addressed in all project states through textbook quality
improvement programs financed by DPEP.



- 90 -
Annex 4
Page 14
State Education Finances
32.      Analyses of trends in education financing and expenditures on education from
the beginning of the Sixth Plan were completed by state teams following guidelines
prepared by NIEPA and the Department of Education. The guidelines called for the
financial data necessary to address the ability of states to finance their shares of the
proposed These were revised several times in light of comments received from NIEPA,
DOE and IDA consultants. Final versions were produced at a workshop in New Delhi
in January, 1994. The data provided by the state reports have been reanalyzed by the
DOE and IDA consultants.
33.      Selected findings.  The findings of the finance studies are summarized in
Annex 15: Public Finances and Education Expenditures in the Six IDA/DPEP States).
Continuing Use of the Studies
34.      Analysis of baseline achievement assessment would continue during the first
year of the project. Results of all beneficiary studies are to be discussed with officials
and community members in each project district. The outcomes of the discussions,
together with study findings, would be used to refine and adjust intervention strategies
in the second year of the project.
35.      Program  design studies have largely been taken into account in DPEP
objectives and work programs. Textbook development programs fully incorporate
recommendations of the study. The in-service teacher training program similarly builds
on findings from the teacher study.  However, the broader implications of the teacher
study for pre-service education, teacher posting policy and teacher career paths would
need widespread discussion with states before change could be considered.



- 91 -
Annex 5
Page 1
INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Development Objectives. Indicators and Monitoring
1.  Measurement of progress towards the achievement of development objectives is a
key feature of the DPEP management structure. To facilitate measurement objectives,
indicators and monitoring procedures have been designed for key selected program
emphases: (a) capacity building at national and state levels; (b) reducing dropout and
improving access; and (c) improving learning achievement.
Capacity Building
2.  National and state capacity building objectives and indicators are shown in Annex
5 Tables 1-7. The indicators focus on observable outcomes of the capacity building
process.  Progress would be monitored by the DPEP Bureau and by IDA through
periodic supervision reports and through the reviews of program implementation carried
out annually as part of the work program and budgeting exercise. In addition, evaluation
studies would be commissioned to assess the impact of key activities.
Reducing Dropout and Increasing Access
3.  The program is intended to reduce overall dropout rate to less than 10 percent over
baseline estimates, and to reduce disparities among gender and social groups to less than
5 percent. Current retention rates and enrollments, by gender and caste, provide the
baselines against which progress would be measured (Annex 5 Table 8). The number
of dropouts to be averted and the number of new places to be created are shown in
Annex 5 Table 9. Progress would be measured through school statistics on an annual
basis.
Improving Learning Achievement
4.  Learning achievement in language and mathematics in the final year of primary
school would increase by 25 percent over baseline estimates. Test parameters are shown
in Annex 5 Tables 5A and 5B. Readers should note that primary education lasts four
years in Assam, Karnataka, Kerala and Maharashtra; and 5 years in Haryana and Tamil
Nadu. Different tests were used for grades 4 and 5, with different numbers of items.
Baseline average raw test scores (number correct) are shown in Annex 5 Table 11 and
the number of students to benefit from improved instruction is estimated in Annex 5
Table 9. The learning achievement figures have been derived from the sample-based
learning achievement studies conducted in all project districts (See Annex 4 for a
complete description of the assessment studies).  Progress in improving learning
achievement would be measured by repeat surveys in the third and sixth year of the project.
