INDIA: District Primary Education Program
DPEP Policies, Strategies and Operational Guidelines
A. PROGRAM
Rationale
1.      Over the past seven years, national policy on primary education has evolved
through three phases: (a) expanding central assistance; (b) targeting external assistance
to backward states through state projects; and (c) targeting incremental central assistance
to backward districts. During the last and present phase of development, the GOI
decided to substantially expand external assistance for primary education. Experience
with single state projects in Andhra Pradesh (ODA), Bihar (UNICEF) and Uttar Pradesh
(IDA), while satisfactory, had also convinced the Department of Education that a strong
intermediary organization at the national level was needed to finance and monitor a
rapidly expanding investment program and to provide a common framework for the
utilization of external assistance. The District Primary Education Program (DPEP) has
been established for these purposes.
2.      The proposed DPEP was discussed in late 1992 and early 1993 at regional
meetings of State education authorities. State response was positive.  Following the
discussions, in April 1993 the GOI issued guidelines for the DPEP; the Guidelines were
also discussed with potential donors.   The program was approved by Cabinet in
December, 1993 as a centrally sponsored scheme of federal grant assistance to states
(See Annex 8 for a complete chronology of the development of the program).
3.      In sum, the program would operate as a national intermediary organization,
appraising, financing and supervising sub-projects proposed and implemented by state
governments.
Strategy
4.      Objectives. The program has five objectives based on the National Policy on
Education (1992):
a.      to build national, state and district institutional capacity for planning,
management, training, and research and development i support of
primary education development;
b.      to  reduce  differences  in  enrollment dropout and  learning
achievement among gender and social groups to less than 5
percent;



- 124 -
Annex 6
Page 2
c.      to reduce dropout rates for all students to less than 10 percent;
d.      to increase average primary learning achievement by 25 percent over
measured baseline levels; and (d) increasing access. Priority will be
given to female, scheduled caste and scheduled tribe students.
e.      to provide, according to national norms, access for all children to
primary education classes (I-V) in primary schools wherever
possible, or its equivalent through nonformal education.
5.       Targeting. The GOI is following the targeting agreements under the Program
to Strengthen the Social Safety Net (Credit 2448-IN) in designing the DPEP. The
literacy criterion for selecting districts would be the average national female literacy rate
of 39 percent; those districts with female literacy rates near to or below this average
would be selected for initial investment. The program would cover all below average
districts in a phased manner. In addition, districts where literacy campaigns have
generated increased demand for primary education would also be eligible for financing.
relatively advanced states.
6.      In principal, states with good performance records could initiate activities in
all their districts over a 10-15 year period. Districts with below average female literacy
rates would get early priority. Once action is underway to improve primary education
in all districts, attention could be shifted to upper primary education. In addition, in line
with the quality improvement objectives, states could propose quality enhancement
activities for primary and upper primary education where enrollments had reached
acceptable levels.
7.       Building Institutional Capacity.  National management capacity would be
established through the creation of a DPEP Bureau in the Department of Education,
supported by management and technical consultant support services.  Capacity for
national technical assistance to states for curriculum and materials development, in-
service teacher training and educational planning and management would be developed
through a network of national and state education institutions. Program research and
evaluation capacity would be developed under the leadership of a newly established
function for Program Evaluation, Research and Studies (PERS) located at the National
Council for Educational Research and Training. PERS would help develop educational
research capacity in national and state education, social science and economics research
institutions, and draw on these capacities to carry out DPEP evaluations and studies
through contracts and collaborative research programs.
8.       The program  offers states the opportunity to establish State Institutes of
Educational Management and Training (SIEMT) modeled on the institution of the same
name developed by the UP Basic Education Project. SIEMTs would have central



- 125 -
Annex 6
Page 3
responsibility for developing planning and management capacities in states. States can
also apply for support for capacity development in textbook and learning materials
preparation, publication and dissemination; teacher in-service training; and educational
research and evaluation.
9.       Program Interventions. While variations in issues and strategies for reducing
dropouts, improving learning achievement, and increasing access are expected to emerge
across districts and states, the DPEP would emphasize the following elements:
a.       Supervision of schools by community organizations to reduce teacher
absenteeism and increase student attendance;
b.       Early childhood education through coordination with ICDS where
possible and through new programs elsewhere;
c.      In-service teacher training at the school and school cluster level
focused on teaching reading and arithmetic, and on teaching in
multi-grade settings;
d.       Training for head teachers and other measures to improve school
management.
e.       Simplified and improved student learning materials, as well as
establishment of school libraries;
f.       Ensuring free textbooks for girls, and SC/ST students;
g.       Ensuring availability of learning aids;
h.       Repair and improvement of school facilities, including water supply
and latrines.
1.       Training for supervisors and other managerial staff.
j.       Construction of needed schools and classrooms;
k.       Opening of nonformal education centers.
10.      Nonformal primary education improvements would include attention to more
effective instructional models, improvement in logistics and training for instructors, and
an enhanced role for communities in supervision and management.



- 126 -
Annex 6
Page 4
11.      Strategies  to  strengthen  education  for girls would  include  women's
empowerment and education, training in gender sensitivity, elimination of gender bias
in learning materials, enhanced role for women in village education committees, and
awareness campaigns.
12.      A variety of initial intervention strategies for Scheduled Tribe (ST) children
would be launched by district implementation teams at the beginning of the program.
Experience with these strategies would be fed into a Program of Special Emphasis on ST
Education, which would be established during the second year of the project to finance
research and development activities aimed at improving the availability of textbooks and
other learning materials in tribal languages, increasing the number of tribal teachers, and
identifying and disseminating most effective intervention strategies.
13.      District Institutes of Education and Training (DIETS) would be strengthened
through equipment and staff development to enable them to provide better technical
support for primary education programs.
B. OPERATIONAL PROCEDURES
14.      The key elements of GOI DPEP operations are:"
a.       States would participate on a voluntary basis.
b.       States must submit proposals that meet program guidelines to gain
access to the program;
c.       State proposals should be built on district action plans prepared by
district teams with widespread popular participation.
d.       Eligible states would be financed annually on the basis of
performance reviews and plans and budgets for the subsequent year.
States or districts not meeting performance standards as measured by
achievement of physical and disbursement targets, as well as overall
management performance, would be assisted in taking corrective
action. If results are not satisfactory, they would be dropped from
the program.
e.       In the initial phase, states would be financed for five to seven years,
with work beginning in three to five districts in each state and on
state institutional capacity.
See Annex 7 for a detailed assessment of DPEP management structures and processes, including flow of funds.



- 127 -
Annex 6
Page 5
f.       Program  expansion to additional districts in participating states
would depend on the findings of a review in the second year of the
program.   Assuming  positive  findings, proposals would  be
entertained for additional districts and state-level activities, as well
as for expansion of pilot activities in districts with previous DPEP
financing.
g.       Participating states would have to covenant with the GOI and donors
to make program resources fully additional to state budgets, and to
fully sustain the recurrent costs of investments.
15.      States would be required to establish a Registered Society to receive and
disburse central assistance and to manage program activities. Such Societies are often
established by the GOI and by State governments to protect development program
resources from diversion to other areas of state expenditures and to ensure flexible
implementation arrangements. The approach is currently being used in donor assisted
projects, including the IDA basic education project and a US$300 million family planning
project in Uttar Pradesh, the UNICEF basic education project in Bihar, and SIDA-
assisted projects in Rajasthan.
Categories of Assistance
16.      States and districts would be eligible to propose activities consonant with the
state and district categories of assistance, consistent with program eligibility and financial
criteria (Attachments I and II).
17.      State-Level Activities to Build Institutional Capacity. Eligible activities include
those which would improve:
a.       the efficiency  of state textbook preparation, publication and
dissemination.
b.       the effectiveness in-service and pre-service primary teacher training
and education;
c.       the effectiveness of educational research and evaluation;
d.       the capacity of state agencies for research and training in educational
planning, management, monitoring and assessment.



- 128 -
Annex 6
Page 6
18.      District-Level Activities to Decentralize Primary Education Improvement
Programs. Eligible activities include those which would improve:
a.       district capacity for program management, supervision, monitoring
and evaluation.
b.      the quality of primary formal and nonformal education in order to
reduce dropout and increase learning achievement.
c.       access to primary formal or nonformal education.
d.       targeting of support for access to and benefit from primary education
for girls, SC and ST students.
Procedures
19.      Application. States may apply for initial or continuing awards following the
procedures outlined in DPEP Guidelines. Applications must conform to DPEP Program
Criteria (Attachment I) and Financial Guidelines (Attachment II). In summary, the
process consists of:
a.       Identification of districts according to DPEP criteria.
b.       Approval of district selections by DPEP.
C.       Appointment (if not in place) of District Planning Teams
d.       Appointment (if not in place) of State Planning Teams
e.       Preparation of preliminary district plans/proposals and cost estimates
for eligible activities.
f.       Preliminary appraisal of district proposals by DPEP.
g.       Preparation of state proposals for capacity building
h.       Appraisal by DPEP
i.       Appraisal by donors as appropriate
20.      Sub-project Appraisal. DPEP staff and consultants would appraise state and
district proposals for sub-projects against program policies and technical, financial and
managerial criteria.   Three missions would be conducted for each state: (1) at the



- 129 -
Annex 6
Page 7
initiation of project preparation; (2) on completion of draft proposals; (3) on completion
of revised proposals.
21.      Sub-project Supervision. DPEP supervision missions would visit each state and
a sample of districts twice annually, preparing supervision reports on all elements of
program implementation. Reports will be made available to all donor agencies.
22.      Performance Monitoring. Program perfornance would be monitored annually
on the basis an Annual Report developed from DPEP and donor supervision reports and
information from the project monitoring system.
23.      Periodic progress reviews would be built around sample-based learning
achievement studies developed by IDA consultants. The methodology has been tested
in Tamil Nadu and used for baseline beneficiary assessments in three initial districts in
Uttar Pradesh.  The GOI has made use of these surveys a requirement of state
participation in the national program (both to establish baselines and to measure
progress). While the first program review is unlikely to show much progress on
attendance and achievement, these data in subsequent years would provide the GOI with
an objective way to assess relative performance of the states (along with managerial
reviews and financial audits).
24.      The first phase of development of a computerized project monitoring system
has been completed. The initial system monitors planned activities, physical targets,
budget allocations, and expenditures at national, state and district levels. The system was
used by the GOI and IDA at appraisal.
25.      Expansion. Once the program is in place, with satisfactory progress, the three
states with currently operating donor-assisted projects could be taken up for subsequent
expansion.  A preliminary targeting matrix and program  schedule is attached as
Attachment III. As shown, as many as ten states in addition to the four with current
donor-assisted programs could participate. With the exception of Assam, the Northeast
States would initially be excluded for security reasons, as would the Punjab and Jammu
and Kashmnir.
Financing Requirements
26.      The program would finance institutional development, curriculum and textbook
revision, in-service teacher training, educational materials, incremental salaries, facilities
improvements, and limited school construction.
27.      Preliminary estimates place the total financing requirement to cover an
estimated 300 districts (in addition to the 38 districts already underway with donor
assistance) at US$ 3.0 billion over twelve years (at an average base cost of US$ 10



- 130 -
Annex 6
Page 8
million per district, including costs of state-level investments).  The commitments
required to fully fund new district programs initiated in Phase 1 of the program (the
balance of the Eighth Plan Period) are estimated at US$ 1.1 billion.  An Eighth Plan
incremental allocation of Rs. 1,950 core (US$ 650 million) has been made by the
Planning Commission to support the program. This amount is roughly equivalent to the
total of disbursements that would be made during the four years remaining in the Eighth
Plan. The balance of the commitment of Phase 1 commitment (US$ 500 million) would
need to be met in the Ninth Plan, along with the investment in additional districts, for
a total Ninth Plan commitment of US$ 2.35 billion.
28.     The GOI expects to be able to finance its share of the program with external
assistance. Under the SSN program, the EEC has provided provide approximately
US$200 million of grant assistance for the program, which the GOI will earmark for 20
districts. ODA has agreed to an expansion of assistance to Andhra Pradesh, and to
additional financing for West Bengal, under DPEP.



- 131 -
Annex 6
Page 9
Attachment I
INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Program Eligibility Criteria
Criteria           Evidence
Equity focus       Focus on districts with low female literacy rates
Baseline beneficiary studies
Specific strategies for girls, SC/ST students
Decentralization   Action plans and budgets developed at the district level
Investment in district-level institutional capacity
Participatory      Village leadership, NGOs, District, Block, and School level
Planning           personnel involved in program planning through consultations &
workshops
Technical          Strategies are based on empirical evidence or experience,
Feasibility        evidence or experience, preferably in India
Managerial        Implementation by a registered society empowered to make
Feasibility       financial, staffing and project design decisions
Plan for MIS development that meets GOI requirements
Acceptable plans for development of enhanced State capacity for
textbook development, teacher and staff training, student learning
assessment, and program evaluation.
Financial          Program resources are a net addition to normal growth of
Feasibility        State Plan allocations for education as a whole and primary
education specifically
Full annual program costs are included in annual State budgets.
Annual recurrent costs of the investment are shown to be
sustainable on State Non-plan budgets at the end of the project



- 132 -
Annex 6
Page 10
Attachment II
INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Financial Criteria
1.      In addition to conforming to program Categories of Assistance and meeting
Program Eligibility Criteria, proposals for state and district action programs must meet
additional Financial Criteria. These have been designed by the Department of Education
to ensure that program resources are additional to state financed investment programs,
to manage costs and to ensure that financing is directed toward program priorities.
These criteria have evolved during the development of the program, and may be modified
from time to time in light of experience, with the approval of the DPEP Program
Approval Board.
Additionality Criteria
2.       DPEP would not finance:
a.       Salaries for sanctioned but unfilled posts;
b.       Salaries of posts that should have been created by states in order to
meet state norms, such as the student teacher ratio;
c.       Salaries of posts already financed but transferred to DPEP activities;
3.       DPEP would finance salaries for newly appointed program staff on a declining
basis, beginning with 90 percent in the first two years, 80 percent for the third, fourth
and fifth years, and 65 percent in the seventh year of the project.
4.       Teacher salaries would be financed on the same declining basis in new schools
and classrooms constructed by the project and, from the third year of the project onward,
in schools in project districts where enrollment increases over baseline figures and state
teacher: student ratios justify the appointment.
5.       As a general principal, DPEP funds cannot be used to substitute for
expenditures states and districts would otherwise make. For example, if states now
provide textbooks free of charge to all students, DPEP would not finance textbook
printing and distribution costs for eligible project districts.
Cost Management Criteria
6.       Administrative Overheads. Program administration costs should not exceed six
percent of total program costs for a given state.



- 133 -
Annex 6
Page 11
7.       Incentives. DPEP would not finance incentive schemes such as free uniforms,
mid-day meals, attendance incentives, or cash awards for student or school performance.
Only provision of free textbooks for girls, SC and ST students would be financed in
project districts where states do not already have such a scheme.
8.       DPEP would finance awards programs for schools organized at the block level
with a view toward promoting competition among schools in the enrollment and retention
of girls and SC/ST students.  Awards must be used by the school for facilities
improvements or the acquisition of learning materials.
9.       Vehicles. DPEP would finance vehicles as follows:
a.       State level: one vehicle for state project director, two vehicles for
the project pool, one for the director of SIEMT, and one for the
SIEMT pool;
b.       District level: one vehicle for district program director, vehicles for
the program pool calculated at one for every four blocks in the
district; and one vehicle for the DIET to be used in providing
support to block and cluster training activities.
10.      Equipment.  DPEP would finance equipment at state, district block and
community levels as justified by state and district proposals. In initial stages of the
program, no televisions or video players/recorders would be financed below the level of
DIETS.
11.      Convergence of services. Activities and processes that promote convergence
and coordination between primary education and other social programs, such as ICDS
and health services, would be eligible for financing. DPEP would not finance the
expansion of ICDS anganwadi centers, but would finance early childhood education
(ECE) centers where ICDS centers are not available. Proposals for ECE centers should
be coordinated with state plans for ICDS expansion.
Program Priorities Criteria
12.     Intensive Application of Resources. The DPEP would finance the development
and improvement of key state education capacities, such as MIS development,
improvement in procedures for textbook and learning materials development, teacher in-
service training, etc., according to program Categories of Expenditure. However, the
costs of district-level implementation of these systems will be limited to eligible project
districts.



- 134 -
Annex 6
Page 12
13.     Civil Works Limitations.  To ensure substantial investment in quality
improvement, DPEP financing for civil works, including renovations, toilets, water
supply and electrification, block and cluster buildings and expansion of state physical
capacity is limited to 24 percent of total state project costs. Office space for DPEP
activities would be financed on a small scale only, and then within the existing facilities
of state education agencies. DPEP would not finance civil works for aided or private
schools, NGOs or other associations or groups.
14.     Residential schools. Proposals for the construction of residential primary
schools for ST and other disadvantaged students would be considered for eligibility from
the second year of the program onwards. Proposals would be required to include
evidence of demand from communities, school maps showing the location of the
proposed schools in relation to underserved comrnunities, and independent evaluations
of the effectiveness of similar existing schools.
15.     Community and School Empowerment. DPEP would finance grants to Village
Education Committees in the amount of Rs. 2000 annually for local purchase of learning
materials, furniture, teaching-learning equipment, student health checkups and other
environment building activities. DPEP would also finance annual grants of Rs. 500 per
teacher for the purchase of consumable supplies and development of low-cost teaching
aids. DPEP would not finance standardized packages of teaching-learning equipment,
furniture and other instructional materials for schools.
16.     Nonformal Education. To encourage continuing improvement in models for
nonformal education, DPEP would finance the development of alternative models and
materials as well as implementation of nonformal education in accordance with the
present GOI scheme in states not yet covered by that scheme.
17.     Phasing New Activities. States and districts may propose core activities for
which there is very little local experience (although relevant experience may be found in
other states). An example is early childhood education. In such cases, DPEP would
finance systematic start-up activities, and evaluations, prior to agreeing to finance
replication and expansion.
18.     Innovations. In order to encourage innovations at all levels, DPEP would
finance innovation funds at district, state and national levels. Continued funding for
innovations wold depend on performance, as reviewed annually.
a.      Districts. A fund of Rs. 100,000 would be established in each
annual budget. Proposals for use of the Fund would be approved by
the district unit of the State implementation society. The cost of any
single proposal should not exceed Rs. 100,000, and the duration
should not exceed one year.



- 135 -
Annex 6
Page 13
b.      States. A fund of Rs. 2,000,000 would be provided annually at to
the State Implementation Society. Proposals would be approved by
the Executive Committee of the Society. The cost of any single
proposal should not exceed Rs. 500.000 and the duration should not
exceed two years.
c.      National. An annual fund of Rs. 10,000,000 will be administered
by the DPEP Bureau for larger proposals from districts, states or
national organizations. Proposals would be approved by the DPEP
Project Approval Board. The cost of any single proposal should not
exceed Rs. 2,000,000. There are no limits of the duration of
activity.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Project Management
1.          The innovative nature of DPEP would require a management structure with
good policy access, substantial authority, a high degree of flexibility in appointing staff
and consultants, strong professional competence, and access to good information on
program activities, accomplishments and problems.
2.          To meet these requirements, the Department of Education would establish
a new organizational unit, the DPEP Bureau, to serve as an intermediary organization
to appraise, finance and supervise state and district sub-projects according to established
criteria and procedures.  The DPEP Bureau would in turn be assisted by an
implementation support group employed under contractual arrangements to the DOE.
Through this contract, the DPEP Bureau would acquire substantial flexibility with respect
to appointment of temporary staff and consultants, including levels of remuneration. The
DPEP Bureau would be governed by a General Council, which would review policies
and progress annually, and a DPEP Project Board, which would be fully empowered for
program, financial and staffing approvals. The general structure is summarized in
Attachment 1.
A. NATIONAL STRUCTURE AND FUNCTIONS
Mission General Council
3.          The General Council would provide policy direction to the DPEP and
facilitate Center-State coordination; it would meet annually and be chaired by the
Minister of Human Resources Development. Members would include the Ministers of
Education of states which implement the program; the Member for Education, Planning
Commission; Secretaries of the central government Departments of Education,
Expenditure, Women and Child Development, and Health, as well as eminent educators
and leaders of prominent NGOs active in basic education. The Joint Secretary of the
DPEP Bureau would be the Member-Secretary of the Council.
DPEP Project Board
4.          The General Council will be assisted by a DPEP Project Board (PB), an
empowered body assigned full financial and administrative powers to implement the
program. The PB would be headed ex officio by the central government Education
Secretary and members would be the Financial Advisor, Department of Education,
Advisor of the Planning Commission, and the Secretary of the Expenditure Department



- 138 -
Annex 7
Page 2
(or the designated representative). The Joint Secretary of DPEP Bureau would be the
Member-Secretary of the PB. The PB would meet quarterly and as requested by the
Chairman.  Fifty percent of members attending would constitute a quorum for all
meetings.
5.          The composition of the PB would empower it to exercise all financial and
administrative powers necessary for program planning and implementation and to ensure
that the necessary governmental approvals are processed expediently within the HRD
Ministry. The broad functions of the PB would be to: (a) recommend policies in regard
to DPEP, (b) consider annual work plans and budgets received from the states and the
DPEP Bureau, which would include annual work plans and budgets for contracts for
program and technical support services, (c) approve changes in norms for new program
components and activities which emerge over the course of implementation, (d) promote
convergence of services, (e) review DPEP progress on a quarterly basis, and (f) provide
guidance to the DPEP Bureau. It would also discharge the functions of the EFC
(Expenditure Finance Committee of the Department of Expenditure) for DPEP projects
that are formulated in the future.
DPEP Bureau
6.          The DPEP Bureau would be fully responsible to the Project Board and
Mission General Council for the implementation of the program within DPEP Guidelines
and in accordance with agreements reached with program donors. The DPEP Bureau
would be headed by a full time Joint Secretary in the Department of Education, reporting
to the Secretary of Education, MHRD. The Joint Secretary, DPEP Bureau (JS-DPEP),
would have overall responsibility for program implementation, including coordination
with state Education Secretaries, within approved Guidelines and annual plans and
budgets. The JS-DPEP would have the authority to approve consultants appointed by
contractors for program and technical services, as well as to request specific services
through terms of reference.
7.          The DPEP Bureau would have the responsibility and authority to appraise,
supervise and evaluate national, state and district sub-projects, to enter into contractual
arrangements and to approve staff to be appointed in the PTG. It would serve as the
secretariat for the Mission Council and Project Board, and would develop and propose
policy and/or program recommendations for approval. It would directly supervise the
operations of the PTG.
8.          Policy Division.  The Bureau would comprise two units: the Policy
Division and the Program Division. The Policy Division would have two Deputy
Secretaries/Directors with appropriate support staff. Much of the background work
relating to policy, servicing of PB, release of finances to states, and overall review of



- 139 -
Annex 7
Page 3
the program would be done within the Bureau itself through the Policy Division. It
would be responsible for policy and coordination with funding agencies, Department of
Economic Affairs, Planning Commission and different ministries and departments in
Government of India such as Department of Rural Development and the Department of
Women and Child Development. It would also be responsible for servicing and
implementing the decisions of PB, release and reimbursement of funds, proper
maintenance of accounts and compliance with the procurement norms of the external
funding agencies.
9.          Program Division.  The Program Division would be staffed by four
officials with the rank of Deputy Secretary, with appropriate support staff. It would
supervise the work of PTG in six areas: planning and pedagogy, appraisal of projects,
supervision, monitoring and MIS, program evaluation and research, and civil works. In
addition, the Program Division would be responsible for overall coordination with states
on implementation of DPEP, with states specifically assigned to each of the four DSs.
The Deputy Secretaries would also represent the Government of India in the state
implementation societies. As more districts are added, additional Deputy Secretaries may
be provided for, from 1995-96 onwards.
DPEP Program and Technical Assistance Services
10.         The DPEP Bureau would be supported by program  and technical
consulting services provided by a consulting firm or firms selected by the DPEP Bureau.
Consultants would carry out annual work programs in supervision, technical assistance,
appraisal, research and evaluation, and project monitoring as approved by the Project
Board, as integral components of the annual work plans and budgets of the DPEP
Bureau.  The annual program and technical services work program would include
financing for these cooperative agreements which would be channeled as pass through
funds in the annual budgets for program and technical consulting services.  The
consulting firm would also respond to requests from the DPEP Bureau for services
arising from implementation of national components, or from state implementation
societies as approved by the DPEP Bureau. Such services would be financed through a
separate line budget arrangement authorized as part of each annual work plan and budget.
Annual budgets for program and technical services should be supplemented as needed by
short-term consultants.
11.         For first 2-3 years of the project (the remaining 3 years of the Eighth
Plan), the program and technical consulting services would be contracted to Education
Consultants India Limited (EDCIL), a public sector undertaking owned by the
Department of Education, to facilitate recruitment of professionals and engagement of
institutional and individual consultants. This flexible structure would enable the DPEP
Bureau to meet additional staffing needs arising from increased responsibilities. Initially,



- 140 -
Annex 7
Page 4
the EDCIL would establish six main units, for: appraisal, supervision, monitoring and
MIS, finance and procurement, civil works, research and evaluation, civil works,
research and evaluation, and technical assistance.  In addition, small units for
administrative and accounting support and for media and communications would be
created. The professionals staffing these units would be contracted following norms and
procedures set out in a contract to be entered into with EDCIL.
12.         The work of EDCIL would be directly managed and supervised by the
Program Division of the DPEP Bureau. Each of the four Deputy Secretaries in the
Program Division would have functional responsibility for corresponding cells in the
EDCIL and, for day to day operations, each deputy secretary would both monitor and
evaluate the performance of his/her respective EDCIL units and represent the unit to the
DPEP Bureau and PB for policy decisions. At the direction of the DPEP Bureau,
EDCIL would establish advisory groups and task forces for each functional area that it
deals with, to guide and review its activities.
13.         Appraisal Cell. Under the supervision of the DPEP Bureau, the Appraisal
unit's staff and consultants would provide on-site appraisal for state and district
proposals. The anticipated appraisal workload would be approximately 20 districts per
year. Each state would receive appraisal missions: (a) at the initiation of sub-project
preparation; (b) on completion of draft proposals; and (c) on completion of revised
proposals. The team would have expertise in educational financing, educational planning
and management, statistics, civil works, in-service training, community participation,
program management, special programs for women/ girls and SC/ST, and school
effectiveness, and would prepare terms of reference for and engage expert consultants
as needed.
14.         Supervision Cell. The unit would organize and staff DPEP participation
in bi-annual joint supervision missions with program donors. In addition it would
organize two bi-annual DPEP supervision mission to each participating state.
Additionally, this cell would assist DPEP Bureau state coordinators to review procure-
ment and disbursement activity, and prepare reports for funding agencies.
15.         Monitoring and MIS Cell. Under the supervision of a Deputy Secretary
in the DPEP Bureau, this unit would manage (a) the technical assistance program to
develop the school statistics MIS and (b) the project monitoring system.  It would
prepare monitoring reports on program implementation for the States, DPEP Bureau and
donors. It would also be responsible for preparing a quarterly DPEP newsletter, with
inputs from the DPEP Bureau and the other Technical and Professional Cells. As the
workload would increase over time, the staffing would expand commensurately. A core
staff of two technical experts and two research assistants is envisaged, with one research
assistant added for every additional 20 districts. The cell would also house an electronic



- 141 -
Annex 7
Page 5
data processing unit (EDP), which would be staffed by a system analyst/senior
programmer and three data entry operators.
16.         Finance and Procurement Cell. This cell would be responsible to the
DPEP Bureau for managing national procurement and supervising procurement by states
and districts according to donor guidelines. It would also monitor the advance of funds
and expenditures, prepare quarterly disbursement requests, and ensure the timely
completion of audit reports. It would initially be staffed by an experienced procurement
officer and an accounting officer with experience with World Bank-assisted projects.
17.         Civil Works Cell. This unit would be responsible for identifying and
disseminating best practices in school construction designs and materials, appraisal of
State and district civil works proposals, and supervise construction ensuring conformity
with national norms and safety standards.  Staff from this unit would join regular
supervision missions. The unit would be headed by a senior Architect/Engineer, who
would be supported by two engineers.
18.         Research and Evaluation Cell. Cooperative agreements for research and
evaluation in primary education would be managed by the cell for Program Evaluation
and Research, under the supervision of the DPEP Bureau. Staff and consultants in the
cell would provide technical oversight and review of the work done under the cooperative
agreement. It would also prepare terms of reference for independent studies on requests
from the DPEP Bureau, and supervise and evaluate the technical quality of the work
performed under such contracts.
19.         Technical Assistance Cell. This cell would manage cooperative programs
in in-service teacher training, pedagogical development, educational planning and
management and ST education development. Through contracts for consultant services
authorized by the DPEP Bureau, it would also assist states to prepare district programs
and plans for consideration for financing by the DPEP PB.   The cell would also
coordinate request for international technical cooperation under the supervision of the
DPEP Bureau. The cell would be staffed by six full time experts, two full-time
equivalent professional consultants and support staff.
B. STATE LEVEL STRUCTURES
Registered Society
20.         The GOI DPEP would be implemented through a registered society in each
state. Registered societies -- empowered, legal entities registered under the Societies
Registration Act of 1860 -- have been used in India to implement time-bound projects for



- 142 -
Annex 7
Page 6
130 years; they have been used for basic education projects in Bihar (1991), Rajasthan
(1992) and Uttar Pradesh (1993).
21.         Each implementation society would be accountable to a General Council
chaired by the Chief Minister or Education Minister of the State and an Executive
Committee chaired by the Chief Secretary/Education Secretary of the State. Membership
in both bodies would include representatives from other state government departments
and the GOI.
22.         Societies have three main advantages over conventional bureaucratic
structures. First, as para-statal bodies, societies have administrative flexibility combined
with public accountability (see accountability, below). Second, representation of several
Departments of the state government and the central government on the governing bodies
of the society facilitates planning and coordination. Third, financial transactions are
efficient and transparent (see flow of funds, below).  Certificates of registration,
memoranda of association and rules and regulations for DPEP societies in all six states
have been submitted to the GOI.
23.         Registered societies can receive funds from the GOI, state governments
and other donors directly and can disburse funds to the operational level of the project
through bank transfers. Under DPEP, these transfers would be made to district-level
implementing bodies on the basis of annual work programs and budgets.
State-level management structure
24.         According to their memoranda of association, Executive Committees of
state societies are authorized to create posts for management of the project. The actual
management arrangement in each state and district level would vary with local
conditions. Management structures proposed for DPEP have many common features,
however, including small staffs, linkages with State Departments of Education and other
departments, support for accounting and financial management at the state and district
level, and consultancies for accessing expertise in key functional areas. (see Attachment
2 for a typical state management structure).
25.         DPEP societies would employ both staff who are on deputation from other
organizations and those who are directly recruited by the society. The terms and
conditions of employment with the society would be governed by administrative and
service regulations which would be framed under the powers provided in the
Memorandum of Association and Rules and/or Service Regulations of the registered
society.



- 143 -
Annex 7
Page 7
District Level Management
26.          DPEP would be implemented at the district level through units of the state
society as supported by staff appointed by state societies, as provided for in the MOA
and Rules of each state-level registered society. The societies' Executive Committees are
empowered to specify the composition, functions and powers of the district management
structure. While states vary in the specifics of the district level management structures,
they share common features (Attachment 2). District level bodies would interact with
the Panchayati Raj structures -- e.g. the Zilla Parishad (District Board), the Block
Development Committee (BDC) and the village panchayats -- and with Village Education
Committees (VEC), which would play a major role in educational management,
supervision and monitoring at the village/school level. Staff of the Department of
Education posted in the district, including supervisory officials and functional specialists,
would work with the district DPEP units.
27.          District Committee. All districts propose to establish a district DPEP
Committee, headed by the District Collector or Chief Executive Officer, with
representatives from the district education department, non-governmental sector as well
as functional specialists. The district DPEP Committee would review the progress of
DPEP in the district and widen the involvement of participating agencies.
28.          District Task Force. Second, all districts propose to establish a District
Task Force that would be the executive body at the district level, to whom well-defined
powers would be delegated. The Chairman of the District Task Force would be decided
by the State Executive Committee, and its membership would include district level
officials and representatives of interested agencies. The membership would vary, but not
exceed 15.
C. MANAGEMENT PLANNING AND CONTROL
29.          The process for management planning and control is summarized in
Attachment 3. Slightly different procedures would be followed for the preparation and
appraisal of district, state and national sub-projects.
30.          District Sub-Projects. In accordance with the DPEP Guidelines, district
teams are responsible for developing district action plans with widespread popular
participation. By request, national support institutions such as NIEPA or NCERT would
provide technical assistance in the preparation of these AWPBs. After district-level
annual work programs and budgets (AWPB) have been prepared, the state
implementation society reviews these plans and determines whether or not revisions are
needed. If they are, the AWPBs are remanded to the district team(s) for revision; at this



- 144 -
Annex 7
Page 8
stage, national support institutions would be called in to provide support. When the
AWPBs have been suitably prepared, they are combined with the state-level AWPB and
sent forward to the DPEP Bureau for review. The DPEP Bureau then assigns the task
of formal appraisal to the PTG, which recommends either acceptance of the proposal or
revision. If revisions are needed, the process begins again. If no revisions are needed
the DPEP Board reviews the package and either approves or sends the proposal back for
further revision. If the DPEP Board approves the state package, it authorizes the
advancement of funds to the state implementation society; a sample of district AWPBs
would be reviewed by IDA.
31.         State Sub-Projects. State sub-projects for institutional capacity building
would be prepared in accordance with DPEP Guidelines and with assistance from
national support institutions. When they are suitably prepared, state-level AWPBs are
submitted to the DPEP Bureau, which assigns the PTG the task of formal appraisal.
Again, if revisions are needed, the proposals are remanded to the state and technical
assistance is provided through the national institutions and if no revisions are
recommended the AWPB is sent forward to the DPEP Bureau for approval in conjunction
with the entire state package.
32.         National Sub-Projects. National sub-projects for institutional capacity
building would be developed by national support institutions, in accordance with DPEP
Guidelines and existing institutional capacity.  Review of these sub-projects would
ultimately be undertaken by panels of professional peers, organized through PTG, and
recommendations made to the DPEP Bureau regarding approval. Initially, national
technical assistance programs would be implemented by NCERT and NIEPA on a project
basis through memoranda of understanding with the DPEP Bureau following standard
GOI procedures, and through contracts with individuals and other institutions. By
negotiations, the GOI would have prepared and pre-authorized memoranda of
understanding with NIEPA and NCERT to provide national research, development and
technical assistance services for (a) school statistics MIS system development (b) primary
teacher in-service training, and (c) program evaluation research and studies.
D. FLOW OF FUNDS
33.         DPEP Guidelines require that total project costs be shared between the
GOI and the state in the ratio of about 85:15. The GOI share would appear in the
Annual Plan and budget of Department of Education, Ministry of Human Resource
Development, Government of India. The states' contribution would be provided for in
the Annual Plan budget of the Education Departments of the respective states.
34.         GOI to societies. Advances from the GOI to state societies would be made
twice annually. Grants to implementing societies would be made directly through a



- 145 -
Annex 7
Page 9
cheque or demand draft by the Pay and Accounts Section of the GOI Education
Department, with the funds deposited in a bank account established by the
implementation society.  Reimbursement claims from the multilateral and bilateral
agencies would be made by DPEP Bureau based on expenditure statements received from
the implementing society. Because the advances are deposited into the accounts of the
implementation society, unspent balances at the end of the GOI fiscal year (March 30)
do not have to be returned or adjusted against subsequent advances, which is an
important advantage of the implementing societies. In principal, any state or district
fully expending the first advance through exceptional implementation could receive a
larger second advance.
35.         Release of funds to DPEP societies would occur every six months. The
first installment for six months' operating expenditures would be made on May 30; it
would be based on: (a) district and state Annual Work Plans and Budgets (AWPB)
prepared and appraised during the preceding December-January, and (b) evidence of
appropriate use of previously released funds ("utilization certificates" for years 2-7 of the
project). The second instalment of six months' operating expenditures would be made
on January 1 and would be adjusted against actual expenditures from the first advance;
it would be based on: (a) evidence that the state had made its matching release of funds
to the Society, (b) "utilization certificates", and (c) the review by the GOI Education
Secretary of satisfactory progress in implementation of the approved work plans. The
second advance would carry the program until the next June 1, overlapping (by two days)
the availability of date of the first advance for the subsequent year.
36.         Societies to states/districts for personnel. Appointing authorities (in some
cases state governments, in other cases district governments) would be reimbursed by the
state implementation society on a quarterly basis for the salaries of teachers in new posts
resulting from the construction of DPEP financed schools and classrooms, and as
justified by dropout reduction and increased enrollments resulting from DPEP activities.
Reimbursement would be on a declining basis as per IDA norms against lists providing
names of teachers, name of school, date of appointment, and monthly salary. Both state
and district bodies are empowered to receive such reimbursements and to credit them on
the revenue side of their budgets.
37.         Societies to districts, blocks, villages, schools.  Each society would
establish rules and regulations for financial flows in its memoranda of association, rules
and financial regulations. The rules would govern formulation of budget, purchase
procedures, delegation of financial powers, investment of funds, maintenance of accounts
and audit, travel and per diem allowance rules, etc. The society Executive Committee
would be empowered to open bank accounts, authorize a Drawing and Disbursing Officer
to operate the accounts, and delegate financial functions to state, district, block, village
and school levels. The Financial Regulations would govern delegation of financial



- 146 -
Annex 7
Page 10
authority for purchasing goods and services, writing checks against society bank
accounts, and procedures for releasing funds to district level DPEP offices.
38.         Specific mechanisms would vary across states. However, DPEP societies
typically propose to open a bank account in a nationalized bank in which to deposit all
funds received. Societies would also open bank accounts in one of the nationalized banks
at the district level and in banks or post offices at the block and village level. Funds
would be advanced to districts through crossed Bank Drafts to the district-level bank
account; payments to block and village level DPEP cooperating institutions would be
made by crossed bank drafts.
39.         Districts would be advanced funds on the basis of annual work plans and
budgets as approved by the state DPEP society Executive Committee. Funds would be
released in installments after ensuring that the first instalment released has been utilized
satisfactorily.
E. ACCOUNTABILITY AND AUDITING
40.   Accountability and auditing would be ensured through established state and GOI
procedures acceptable to IDA in accordance with the provisions of the Societies
Registration Act 1860, and following standard IDA guidelines for audits of project
accounts and Statements of Expenditure.
Accounts
41.         Society.     All DPEP implementation societies would establish financial
regulations, similar to those established under the Lok Jumbish and Bihar Education
Projects, which detail procedures for the purchase of goods and services, including rules
for purchase, receipts, custody and accounting procedure for stores.
42.         State. All states have developed general financial rules that establish an
overall regulatory framework for the financial management of governmental and quasi-
governmental organizations, including DPEP implementation societies. These rules
provide for the purchase and procurement of goods and services, and include tendering
procedures for obtaining goods and services of acceptable quality at reasonable rates.
For DPEP, procurement, service and financial regulations to be adopted by implementing
societies would be prepared and provided to IDA for review. In addition, each state
government would enter into a Project Agreement with IDA.
43.         The Memoranda of Association of all DPEP implementation societies
(including the UP Basic Education Project) provide that the society shall (a) maintain
proper accounts and other relevant records, and (b) prepare annual accounts comprising



- 147 -
Annex 7
Page 11
receipts and payments and statement of liabilities as may be prescribed by the Registrar
of Societies in that particular state.
Audits
44.         Society.  Internal audit functions would be established in DPEP
implementation societies. The accounts of the society would be audited by an independ-
ent Chartered Accountant and in accordance with the provisions of the Societies
Registration Act 1860. The audited accounts along with a copy of the audit report and
society annual report would be furnished to the Executive Committee, which would
comment and submit it to the state government.
45.         State. The state government would comment on the audit report received
from the DPEP implementing society and would submit it to GOI for acceptance. State
governments would also, as required, place these documents in the state legislatures.
46.         GOI. The accounts of societies would be audited by the Comptroller and
Auditor General (CAG) of India, under the provisions (duties, powers and conditions of
service) of the Comptroller and Auditor General Act, 1971 as amended. The act also
provides for special audits of societies, which would be undertaken as necessary. In
addition, the GOI is required to place the annual report and audited statement of accounts
of the project in the Parliament on an annual basis.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Management Development Plan
1.      The District Primary Education Program (DPEP) has been developed as an
intermediary organization to appraise, finance and supervise sub-projects implemented
by states, districts and national technical resource institutions. Program structures and
procedures, and management capacity, have been developed over an 18 month period
(Annex 8 Table 1). Preparation for the proposed IDA credit has provided an extended
opportunity for learning by doing, enabling national, state and district planning and
management teams to implement the activities required to complete a project preparation
cycle. Technical support for the process was provided by faculty from the National
Institute of Educational Planning and Administration (NIEPA) and the National Council
for Educational Research and Training (NCERT) through formal and informal training.
2.      DPEP preparation and appraisal procedures have been consciously modeled on
those of the World Bank. To become familiar with these procedures, DPEP staff in the
Department of Education participated fully in all stages of the IDA appraisal process.
DPEP Bureau staff completed an independent evaluation of sub-projects at appraisal in
parallel with IDA's appraisal.
Management Readiness
3.      Program implementation had begun by the time of project negotiations. The
DPEP management team had completed an ambitious project studies program, prepared
a national program management structure, and appraised proposals for action programs
in 8 states and 42 districts, as well as three of the anticipated five national technical
assistance programs. All legal actions needed to establish program structures at national,
state and district levels had been completed. Procedures for advance of funds and
accounting had been established at all levels of the program.  A contract for technical
and management support services for the DPEP Bureau in the Department of Education
had been pre-approved by IDA and was ready to be signed.
4.      Detailed annual work plans and budgets for the first year of program activity
had been completed for each sub-project, appraised by the DPEP Bureau, and approved
by the DPEP Project Approval Board. A computerized project monitoring system for
tracking physical targets and costs in sub-projects, developed by IDA for appraisal, had
been adapted by the DPEP Bureau and was in use.
5.      Key staff had been appointed in the DPEP Bureau and in all State
Implementation Societies and District Implementation Units. All of the staff in the DPEP
Bureau had been full time members of the DPEP Program Development Team (PDT)



- 154 -
Annex 8
Page 2
during the program development period. At the state and district levels, more than 50
percent of appointed staff had participated in project planning and preparation.
6.      Two tranches of funds had been released to State Implementation Societies,
which had advanced funds to district implementation units. Funds had also been
advanced to institutions implementing national technical assistance programs. Work had
begun in all sub-projects.
Continuing Management Development
7.      The proposed IDA credit would assist in formalizing these organizational
structures and procedures, and in further development of management capacity.
8.      Monitorable actions for continued development of management capacity have
been established for the first year of project activity (Annex 8 Table 1). These include
key actions in program staffing and operations, as well as training in program
management.
9.      IDA  staff based in the Resident Mission would provide continuing
implementation support for the program, including formal training in IDA procedures.
Particular priority would be given in the first year to assistance with the development of
DPEP supervision procedures, procurement and timely preparation of disbursement
requests.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program 1
Education of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes
Background
1.  Members of scheduled caste (SC) and scheduled tribe (ST) groups are
disadvantaged socially, economically and educationally, and have historically been
victims of exploitation and social injustice, despite post independence legislation to
eliminate discrimination. SCs and STs form 15.75 percent and 7.88 percent respectively
of the overall population, but are often both physically and socially isolated from
majority communities.
2.  Several constraints hinder SCs and STs from participating in the education process,
principally their isolated geographic location. Most SC and ST children have parents
who never attended school and who are impoverished. These parents are unable to
afford school expenses or the loss of their children's contribution to the family income.
Internal to the school system are also the problems associated with the teacher's
background (whether SC/ST or non-SC/ST), the school calendar, the medium of
instruction, the supervision/inspectorate, incomplete schools at the primary levels, and
the relevance of curricula and textbooks. These constraints, coupled with weaknesses
in the monitoring and evaluation system as well as the lack of collaboration between
agencies of the Government that are responsible for education and welfare of the SCs and
STs, have contributed to the lack of access to adequate educational facilities, low
enrollment rates, high dropout rates, low level of encouragement to attend schools due
to lack of role models, and low level of literacy among the SCs and STs. Scheduled
tribes, particularly ST girls are more educationally disadvantaged than SCs. Since
independence, successive governments have taken initiatives to improve the socio-
economic status of SCs and STs, particularly through the institutionalization of special
education programs.
3.  SC children speak regional languages in the home, and thus benefit from attending
integrated school with poor children of other castes.  Improving the quality and
availability of basic educational services in rural schools in targeted districts is the most
effective way of improving their benefits from education. Some ST students also enroll
in integrated schools. A household survey conducted for the project in Assam, for
example, indicates that the families of these children are more assimilated with the
mainstream culture. However, in several project districts, tribals live separately, and
maintain their tribal language and customs. The policy of GOI has been to provide ST
students with instruction in the language of the home in the early primary grades for the



- 160 -
Annex 9
Page 2
60 some languages that are written. Implementation of this policy, however, has been
weakened by lack of resources.
Legal and Policy Framework
4.   Constitutional Framework. Since independence, India has endeavored to promote
education of the weaker sections of its society, namely the SCs and STs.  The
Constitution of India provides for the protection of the interests of the SCs and STs
against any injustice with regard to cultural and educational rights, and the establishment
and admission to educational institutions and grants from State funds in Article 29(1),
29(2) and 30(1), as follows:
"Any section of the citizens residing in the territory of India or any part thereof
having a distinct language, script or culture of its own shall have the right to
conserve same."
"No citizen shall be denied admission into any educational institution, maintained
by the State, or receiving aid out of State funds on grounds only of religion, race,
caste, language or any of them."
"All minorities, whether based on religion or language, shall have the right to
establish and administer educational institutions of their choice."
5.   Furthermore, Articles 45 and 46 provide the following directive principles:
"the State shall endeavor to provide within a period of ten years from the
commencement of this Constitution for free and compulsory education for all
children until they complete the age of fourteen year."
"the State shall promote with special care the educational and economic interests
of the weaker sections of the people, and in particular, of the Scheduled Castes and
Scheduled Tribes, and shall protect them from social injustice and all forms of
exploitation.
6.  The Seventh Amendment of the Constitution Act, 1956, s.21, provides for facilities
for instruction in mother-tongue at the primary stage through the following special
directives:
"It shall be the endeavor of every State and of every local authority with the State
to provide adequate facilities for instruction in the mother-tongue at the primary
stage of education to children belonging to linguistic minority groups; and the



- 161 -
Annex 9
Page 3
President may issue directions to any State as he considers necessary or proper for
the securing or such facilities.
7.  The Sixth Schedule has Provisions for the Administration of Tribal Areas in
selected states that provide for the creation of autonomous District Councils. The
District Council for an autonomous district has the power to establish, construct, or
manage primary schools, dispensaries, etc. in the district, and may, with the previous
approval of the Governor, make regulations for the regulation and control thereof, and
in particular, may prescribe the language and the manner in which primary education
shall be imparted in the district.
8.  Policy Framework. In the context of social justice, the Education Commission in
its 1964-66 report, states:
"One of the important social objectives of education is to equalize opportunity,
enabling the backward or underprivileged classes and individuals to use education
as a lever for the improvement of their condition. Every society that values social
justice and is anxious to improve the lot of the common man and cultivate all
available talent, must ensure progressive equality of opportunity to all sections of
the population. This guarantees the building up of an egalitarian and humane
society in which the exploitation of the weak will be minimized."
9.  The National Policy on Education adopted in 1986 states the following objectives
amongst others: (a) the provision of 5 years of education for all children who attained
the age of 11 by the year 1990; (b) the provision of education for all children up to the
age of 14 by the year 1995; (c) the establishment of a national curricular framework
providing for a common core and other components that would be flexible. The aim of
the common core would be to promote national identity, scientific temper, egalitarianism,
democracy, socialism, secularism, etc.
10. Universal Primary Education (1-5) and the Universal Elementary Education (6-8)
are to ensure that each child: (a) at the specified age of 11 attains the minimum level
of learning for the primary stage of education (UPE - grades 1-5); and (b) at the age
of 14, attains the minimum level of learning prescribed for the upper primary stage of
education (UEE - grades 6-8).
Current Situation in DPEP Districts
11. DPEP aims at expanding and improving primary education in districts that are
targeted as being below the average national female literacy rate. In project states, the
SC population ranges from 11 to 18 percent of the total population, with the national
average being 14 percent. STs constitute less than one percent of the population in



- 162 -
Annex 9
Page 4
Kerala and Tamil Nadu, 4 percent in Karnataka, 6 percent in Maharashtra, and 14
percent in Assam. There are no STs in Haryana. In Kerala and Assam, districts with
high concentration of ST students have been selected for investment. Tribal populations
of the proposed IDA DPEP districts range from about 10,000 persons to about 350,000
persons. The proportions of scheduled tribes in the population range from less than 1
percent to over 50 percent. For example, in Karbi Anglong District of Assam, STs live
in small (fewer than 500 persons) habitations where more than half the population is
tribal, while in Wayanad District in Kerala, STs live in large (more than 2000 persons)
habitations where fewer than 20 percent of the population is tribal.  Attachment I
summarizes, on a district-by-district basis, the demographic picture for STs in IDA
DPEP districts.
DPEP Strategies and Action Plans
12.      The general principal for tribal education supported under DPEP is that ST
children get their fair share of investment. To assist districts plan their programs for
tribals, an extensive tribal targeting study was conducted (a summary of findings is
presented in Annex 4). The study addressed the demography, ethnography and learning
achievement of tribes and tribal students in project districts. District strategies for tribal
education reflect differences in tribal concentrations in districts, from those in which
tribal groups are concentrated in small villages to those in which tribal groups are
integrated with the majority populations.  Plans for tribal education, which were
developed through participatory methods in DPEP districts, differentiate between those
that are non-DPEP strategies (but focus on STs), general infusion strategies that would
benefit the largest numbers of STs in the district, and targeted strategies for STs. Annex
9 Table 1 summarizes district by district, actions proposed in each of these categories.
