INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Girls' and Women's Education
Indicators of Female Educational Status
1.    In most Project districts, educational statistics show that girls and wonmen lag
considerably behind their male counterparts (see Table 1). Indeed, the District Primary
Education Program has selected districts which have the lowest femlale literacy rates in
India. Major objectives of the program are to close the gender gap in primary school
enrolment to less than five per cent during the life of the project, and in literacy over the
long term.
2.    In addition to lower literacy rates, the gender gap in education is characterized
by:
*     lower enrolment of girls in primary school, as well as at higher levels of
the educational system;
*     higher drop-out (i.e. low retention and completion) rates among girls.
Low retention is a more significant problem than low enrolment among
girls compared withl boys; and, on the whole, failure to complete the
primary cycle has a mucLh greater impact oi0 overall levels of education
than does failure to enrol;
significantly larger gaps between girls and boys in Scheduled Caste and
Scheduled Tribe groups than in the school population as a whole;
significantly greater differences between girls and boys in rural areas,
compared with urban areas.
Determinants of Female Education
3.    During Project Preparation, gender studies were conducted in the Project districts
to investigate reasons underlying the differences in girls' enrolment and retention, and
gender inequality in education (see Annex 4). Both 'supply' and 'demand' factors were
revealed to be significant causes of low female education in most districts. On the
demand side, factors that affect girls' schooling significantly include:
parental perceptions that the value of girls' education is low because the
education is irrelevant to the social and economic needs of their daughters'



- 176 -
Annex 10
Page 2
current or future lives. On the whole, the low value attached to femiale
education is a reflection of the low status of women;
*     girls' involvement in sibling care and other domestic work, as well as in
wage work, resulting in high opportunity costs to families of daughters'
educationi; this is especially important among poor families, and is
exacerbated by the above perception of the low value of education;
perceptions that the direct costs of education (fees, textbooks, materials,
clothing, transport) are high, especially for girls;
*     social concerns regarding girls' safety, particularly if they have to walk
long distances or if there are few women teachers; this is especially
important for girls aged 10 and II years, who are approaching puberty,
and those above this age;
*     the practice of early marriage, and hence of withdrawing girls from school
at the age of 10 or II years to prepare them for marriage.
4.    On the supply side, parents report the following as problems which negatively
affect their decisions to send daughters to school:
*     physically inaccessible schools;
*     inflexible school timings;
*     a paucity of female teachers;
*     inadequate infrastrmctural facilities for girls, such as single-sex schools,
or separate toilets;
*     irrelevance of the curriculumii to girls' current or flture lives;
*     inadequate teaching and a lack of remedial teachinig, which result in
parental perceptions that girls must be withdrawn from  school because
they are unable to cope with the curriculum; this is especially important
among illiterate families. It should be noted, however, that the baselinle
assessment studies fouLid very little difference in the actual learning
achievemnents of girls and boys who are in school.
5.    The Project Preparation studies also revealed significant gender biases among
teachers and administrators who believe that girls may be less intelligent than boys,
perform less well, and have bleaker future prospects. Reviews of textbooks and other
teaching-learning materials used in primary schools also revealed gender bias.



- 177 -
Annex l
Page 3
Provisions for Girls' Education in the Project
6.    The preliminary findings of these studies were utilized to plan strategies to
address the problems facing girls' education in the Project districts. All district plans
recognize the existence of gender disparities and the importance of girl's education to
achieving UEE. All have proposed activities to improve girls' enrolmlient and retention,
and some include strategies to improve girls' learning achievemnents as well. Together,
the plans currently include a wide variety of interventions for girls. The attached
diagrams show the wide range of activities proposed in the Project as a whole (Diagram
1), and the more specific proposals of two districts - Belgaulll in Karnataka which has
planned only a few activities (Diagram 2), and Latur in Maharashtra which has several
more (Diagram 3).
7.    During the first year of the Project, as analysis of the study findings is completed,
the strategies for girls will be strengthened through a series of participatory workshops.
They will be integrated into plans which maximnize each district's effort to get girls into
schools and ensure their continlLationi throuighi the primary years. The districts and states
will consolidate their proposals into a comnprehensive and well-synchronized effort to
narrow the gender gap.
8.    In the Project as a whole, several strategies are directed at increasing girls'
enrolment, ensuring continuation in school and completion of the five-year cycle of
primary education, and enhancing their learning achievements. Some activities aim to
address household-level constraints to demanid for female educationi, while others are
focussed on improving education for girls or removing the gender bias in education.
DPEP Guidelines place special emplhasis on, and make provisions for women's
empowerment programls in order to encouLrage girls' education directly, as well as
indirectly by enhancing acdult women's awareness and fuLnctional literacy, and raising the
status of women.
9.    Some specific activities directed at each of these objectives are described below.
10.   Addressing household constraints to demand for girls' education. Awareness
campaigns are to be conducted in most Project districts to promote girls' education as a
priority area for action, educate families about the benefits of girls' education and
motivate them to send daughters to school.
11.   In addition, Village Educationi Coiiirnittees set up in Project districts are expected
to be key instrumnents in increasinig demand for girls' education as well as in achieving
objectives (b) and (c) below. The VECs are to include women members and SC/ST
representatives.  In some districts, VECs will compete for awards for successful
achievement of universal enrolment. Other community groups entrusted with similar



- 178 -
Annex 10
Page 4
responsibilities include Parent-Teachler- Associatioiis, Mother-Teacher Councils, inahila
mandals and other women's organizations.
12.   Increasing girls' enrolnent.  Gender (and other social group) disparities in
enrolment are to be reduced to less than five per cent in the Project districts. To achieve
this, a numiber of strategies have been proposed:
a.     Provision of ECE centres: These centres will have the dual effect of
encoLuraginig  school enrolimienit as a result of 'headstart' pre-school
educationl as well as by providling the day-care arrangements for younger
siblings of childreni (uisually girls) who do not attend school on accoulit of
sibling care responsibilities.
b.     Under the Project, most children will be provided free text-books and, in
addition in some districts/states, girls and SC/ST children (or another
suibset of childcren. e.g. children in Class I) will be giveni materials such
as slates or stationery. ThoLighi not finaniced by IDA, several states in the
Project have provisions for scholarshiips or 'sponsorships' for girls and
SC/ST children, attendlanice allowances, free uniforms, or free mid-day
mieals, which are expected to have positive effects on girls' enrolnent and
conitinuation in school.
c.     Increasing the proportion of female teachers. Different states propose to
achieve this through different means.  For example, Tamil Nadu has
decreed that all primary school teachers should be women, and intends to
achieve this by  appointing  only  womeni to all teachers' positiolis
henceforth. Other states will increase the number of female teachers more
gradually by appointinig women to a given proportion of new teacher posts
and replacemients. Somie states propose to reserve fixed proportions of
places in primary teacher training courses for women.
d.     Non-formal education centres will be established for non-enrolled children
(especially girls), drop-outs and working children.
e.     Construction of new schools especially for girls (in Kerala), or hostels (in
Maharashtra).
13.   Ensuring continuation in school.  The Project is intended to reduice overall
dropout rates to less than ten per cent of baseline estimates. Strategies (b) (i) to (v)
above would also serve to retain girls in school.  In addition, proposals to provide
separate toilet facilities for girls are expected to have an impact on girls' continuation in
schools, as well as those to give awards to girl students.



- 179 -
Annex 10
Page 5
14.   Enhancing learniig achievemilenits. Learninig achievemelits (for both girls and
boys) in language and matilemilatics in the final year of primary school are to be increased
by 25 per cent over baseline estimates. Several districts provide for self-learning
materials, extra tuition after school hours and during holidays, or remedial teaching,
which will address the problem parents and teachers perceive of girls having difficulty
with the curriculum  and dropping out on account of "failure".
15.   Addressing gender bias in education . Strategies incilide:
*     revision of textbooks,
*     curriculumiii reform,
*     gender sensitization of teachers and adminiistrators througil pre-service and
in-service training, and of comImILunity memiibers and groups, other
government fuinctionaries such as ECCE workers, and textbook authors
through orientation workshops, and
appointment of women inspectors to monitor and assist in enhancing girls'
education.
Project Benefits to Girls' Education
16.   As Annex 5 Table 4 shows, the numnber of girls who will benefit from the Project
will be fewer than the nIlliuber of boys. This is becaLise of the current large gaps in
enrolment, which will close only slowly over the Project period. However, during each
year of the Project, the rate at which girls enrolment increases is expected to be higher
than that of boys. Furthermore, the numiiber of "drop-outs averted" among girls will
exceed the number among boys in the Project districts in Haryana, Karnataka and
Maharashtra and will be equivalent in Kerala. This will be achieved because most of the
districts in these states have large gender gaps in enrolment and retention, and will
achieve both higher enrolment and higher retention during the Project period. However,
in Assam and Tamil Nadu, there will be fewer girls among "drop-outs averted" because
the current gender gaps in retention rates are relatively small.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
National and State Implementation Plans
1.          DPEP annual implementation plans at national and state levels focus on
scheduling for key activities and products. Actions and outcomes are shown in relation
to objectives and to anticipated end-of-project status (Annex 5 Development Objectives,
Indicators and Monitoring). Progress against annual implementation plans would be
reported annually, and plans for subsequent years based on progress achieved. First year
implementation plans for national and state components are shown in Annex 11 Tables
1 to 7.
2.          The first year implementation plans of states focus on key start up
activities: (a) staffing and equipping of society offices, (b) putting the education statistics
MIS system in place, (c) conducting initial workshops and design activities for the
development of Minimum Levels of Learning curricula preparatory to textbook revision
in subsequent years, and (d) completing a first round of development activity would be
undertaken for in-service teacher training materials development and training of master
trainers.  These core activities have been coordinated through national planning
workshops, and thus states have provided similar schedules and modalities of work in
these sub-components.
3.          First year state activities are linked to training and technical support
provided from the initial set of national technical assistance programs, the School
Statistics MIS program managed by the DPEP through NIEPA, the Training Resource
Group at NCERT, and the Program Evaluation Research and Studies Program, also at
NCERT. The implementation plans also identify the additional national technical
assistance programs that would support state activities from the second year of the project
onward.

lINDIA: District Prunary Education Program
International Cooperation
1.   Central to DPEP is improvement in the quality of primary education, in
management, training, pedagogy, community participation and research and
evaluation. While substantial professional expertise is available from indigenous
institutions, the Indian education system in general and DPEP in particular would be
enriched by international exchange of comparative experience.
2.   Focal Areas. The GOI has identified nine focal areas corresponding broadly to
the main themes of DPEP national technical assistance programs where comparative
experience would be valuable:
a.   Multi-grade Teaching
b.   Reading and Mathematics Teaching and Learning
c.   Education Planning and Management
d.   School Management and Effectiveness
e.    Alternative and Nonformal Education
f.    Development of Instructional Materials
g.   New Methods of Pedagogy
h.   In-service Training
i.    Educational Research and Evaluation
3.   Programs. In these areas, and in others that could be identified as needs arise,
the GOI would seek to foster two-way international exchange of experience and
expertise. Four main programs for cooperation would be undertaken:
a.   International Conferences and Seminars in India. Travel and per diem
would be provided to enable international scholars and experts to
participate in conferences and seminars organized in India by the DPEP
Bureau, cooperating research agencies, or participating state
implementation societies. A maini function of these conferences would
be to encourage sharing of comparative research experience, with the



- 236 -
Annex 13
Page 2
research carried out in preparation for startup of DPEP receiving major
attention in the first two years of the program.
b.    International Conferences arid Seminiar-s Abroadl. Travel and per diem
would be provided to enable Indian officials and experts to participate
regularly in major international conferences and semlinars relevant to the
focal areas of the program.
C.    International Training Programs and Study Tours. Project staff woluld be
sponsored to participate in short-term international training Courses. In
addition, for course found to be especially useftil, DPEP would sponsor
cooperative arrangeimients between interniational arid national institutions
to adapt trainiing programs and muaterials for delivery in India on a larger
scale. Study tours would be uLl(lertakeni in the first year of the program
to review programii iinpleieentatiori, researclh ar(i developmiient activities,
and possible training progranis in other couLntries.  Indian scholars coLIld
be supported as research fellows in cooperating organizationls. Travel,
per diem and tuition costs would be provided.
d.    Technical Cooperation in Programii Development. Individuals or teamis
of experts would be engaged to participate in DPEP-sponsored research,
developmenit and training activities in India. Travel, per cierii and fees
would be provided by DPEP.
4.    Twinning arrangenierits with agencies arid Luniversities abroad would be
considered when appropriate. These woUnld generally involve excharnge of staff for
specific purposes.
5.    Planning and Approval. The international cooperation program would be
planned annually. A committee of experts chaired by the Joint Secretary (DPEP)
would consider proposals from individuals, institutioins or DPEP state implenientation
societies. Proposals Could also originate in the DPEP Buireaui. Proposals to invite
international participant and consUltants would rnormally be considlered as part of this
annual planning process, but could be approved on an as needed basis by the PB. All
proposals would be received by AugLust of each year to be reviewed by the DPEP
committee, with PB approval by October.  IDA Guidlelines for Fellowships anid
Consultant Services would be followed.
6.    The preliminary first year work program for international cooperation is shown
in Annex 13 Table 1. This would be reviewed and placed before the Project Board
on an acceleratecd schedLule.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Public Finances and Education Expenditures in the Six IDA/DPEP States
1.    The DPEP Guidelines include three financial criteria:
(a)   states will be required to contribute around 15 percent of total project
costs
(b)   program resources are to be a net addition to normal growth of state plan
allocations for education as a whole and primary education specifically
(c)   annual recurrent costs of the investment are to be shown to be sustainable
on state non plan budgets at the end of the project.
Initially, the DPEP is being supported by IDA in 23 districts across 6 states. The
intention is to eventually cover many more districts in these states. This would create
additional financial demands on the state governments.
2.    This annex analyzes the financial capacity of DPEP states to meet the criteria and
projected expansions.  It concludes that:  (a) all state govemments will be able to
contribute their 15 percent share of project costs, (b) Assam and Tamil Nadu might have
difficulties meeting the additionality criteria, (c) all states should be able to sustain
project costs in the initial project districts, but (d) expanding DPEP to additional districts
could place sustainability at risk, particularly in Assam and Tamil Nadu. Uncertainties
related to project financing stem from current patterns of public finance (Assam and
Tamil Nadu) and a projected decline in the growth of transfers from the center to all
states. Growth in state revenues resulting from a stronger economy plus a projected
restructuring of state public finances could result in additional resources for DPEP.
A. EDUCATION FINANCE IN INDIA
Sources of Education Finance
3.    Education is financed from  three sources:  the central government, state
governments and households. Government expenditures on education comprise central
and state, plan and non-plan expenditures. Plan expenditures finance new activities and
non-plan expenditures finance existing activities. The central government provides no
non-plan support to primary education.
4.    Total government expenditures. In 1991/92, total (central plus state, plan and
non-plan) government expenditures on education in India amounted to 3.3 percent of
GDP, a share substantially lower than several other highly populated countries in the
region. The share of total budgetary resources devoted to education is also relatively



- 246 -                         Annex 15
Page 2
low. While the share has increased over recent years from 9.8 percent in 1985/86 to
10.6 percent in 1991/92, it is still below the average of 12.2 percent for all other low
income countries and well below the 18-20 percent recorded such countries as Malaysia
and Thailand.
5.    The distribution of total government education expenditure by level changed only
slightly between 1980 and 1992. Between 1985 and 1992, the share for elementary
education (which includes both lower and upper primary, and in most states covers eight
years of schooling) was 45 percent of the total. Again, comparisons with other countries
in the region utilizing common definitions of primary and secondary schooling suggest
that the share for primary in India is low.
6.    The Eighth Plan (1992-97) envisages a sharp change in the pattern of total
government plan expenditures across the education sector, in two respects. First, total
plan expenditures on education are expected to be 2.5 times higher than those in the
Seventh Plan.  Second, elementary education will receive a higher share of plan
expenditures.  Compared to the period 1985-92, plan expenditures for elementary
education are projected to increase from 37 to 47 percent with consequent falls in
secondary (from 24 to 18 percent) and higher (16 to 8 percent) education; Eighth Plan
expenditures on elementary education are expected to be 3.5 times higher than those in
the Seventh Plan.
7.    Central government.  In recent years, central government expenditures for
education have averaged around 1.3 percent of total central government expenditures.
The central government provides less than 10 percent of total government education
expenditure, all of which for elementary education is plan expenditure.  Central
government plan expenditures for elementary education have increased. In the Sixth Plan
(1980-85), elementary education was allocated 11.7 percent of the total, a figure that
increased to 24.2 percent in the Seventh Plan (1985-90) and is projected to increase to
38.6 percent in the Eighth Plan.
8.    State government. State governments provide over 90 percent of total government
education expenditure. The overall share of state domestic product allocated to education
has increased from 2.8 percent in 1978/79 to 4.0 percent in 1990/91. In the 1990/91,
this share ranged from 3.0 percent in Haryana to 6.3 percent in Kerala. As a share of
total state government expenditures, education expenditures averaged 18.4 percent in
1990/91, up from 15.4 percent in 1980/81, an ranged from 14.9 percent in Haryana to
24.6 percent in Kerala. Expenditure per capita ranged from Rs155 in Uttar Pradesh to
Rs271 in Kerala.
9.    Reflecting the division of responsibilities, a greater share of state government
expenditure (49 percent in 1991) is allocated to elementary education than is the case for
central government expenditures and a slightly lower share is allocated to higher
education. The latter is the only level whose share has fallen over the last decade. The
pattern of Plan expenditures in particular has clearly shifted towards elementary



- 247 -                         Annex 15
Page 3
education since 1970 and this is projected to be repeated during the Eighth Plan (1992-
97). Again there are substantial differences in the shares of education expenditure by
state with, for instance, elementary taking 61 percent of the share in Madhya Pradesh and
31 percent in the Punjab.
10.   Households. A third major source of education finance is households, through
direct and indirect contributions to public and private schools. According to the Fifth
All India Survey for 1986, 5 and 9 percent of primary and upper primary enrollments
respectively were in private aided schools. In the larger cities, unaided schools are more
evident. In Delhi, for instance, 14 and 8 percent of primary and upper primary children
were in these schools.  Households also make both direct and indirect financial
contributions towards children's schooling in government schools. National Sample
Survey data for 1986/87 provide some estimates of overall household education
expenditure. In elementary education the average annual expenditure was reported to be
Rs86 in government schools and Rs217 in aided schools. These figures can be compared
with the average unit cost of elementary education across the six IDA/DPEP states of
Rs441 in that year.
State Government Finances and Economic Adjustment
11.   State governments fund over 90 percent of the public costs of education in India
and virtually all primary and secondary education. Any consideration of education
finance, therefore, has to be seen in the wider context of state finances.
12.   Revenue account deficits. Between 1980/81 and 1986/87 a deficit on the all-
states' revenue (recurrent) account occurred in only one year. Since then, dissaving on
a considerable magnitude has occurred each year: (Rs crores)
1987-88     -       1,088       1990-91      -     5,309
1988-89     -       1,807       1991-92     -      5,651
1989-90     -       3,682       1992-93      -     4,116 (revised estimate)
While falling in 1992-93, the deficit is predicted by the Reserve Bank to increase once
again in 1993-94.
13.   These deficits together with capital expenditures and net lending constitute the
gross fiscal deficit. This has increased even more dramatically since the early 1980s and
is expected to increase significantly over 1993/94: (Rs crores)
1980-81   -    3,713            1992-93   -   19,988 (revised estimates)
1990-91   -   18,787            1993-94   -   23,706  (budget estimates)
1991-92   -   18,900



- 248 -                          Annex 15
Page 4
14.   Equally disturbing, the proportion of overall borrowing which covered deficits of
capital expenditure fell from 86 percent in 1980 to 49 percent in 1990-91 implying an
increasing necessity to borrow for revenue (basically recurrent) expenditures. The budget
deficits have led to increased loans and indebtedness. The outstanding debts of state
governments grew from Rs21,600 crores, equal to 17.6 percent of GDP, in 1980 to
Rs91,000 crores equal to 23.0 percent of GDP in 1990. As a result, levels of interest
and capital repayments are high and growing. For all states combined these were equal
to 15.7 percent of non Plan expenditures in 1989-90 rising to 17.9 percent in 1991-92.
15.   In the current economic climate, the option of depending on increasing central
transfers to finance both investments and recurrent operations is being foreclosed given
the fragility of the central government's own fiscal position: the answers are to increase
the tax base and to increase non tax revenues through particular increases in user
charges. Across states in general, fiscal correction is required if governments'
developmental role, including the provision of primary education, is to be maintained or
increased.
16.   Revenue accounts in IDA/DPEP states. As indicated above, across all states there
have been deficits on the revenue account each year since 1987-88, rising in all but one
of these years. In table I the revenue account surplus/deficits for the six IDA DPEP
states are presented for 1991/92 (actual), 1992/93 (revised) and 1993/94 (budget). They
are also shown as a share of total receipts. Only Assam, dependent on central transfers
to the extent of 48 percent of revenues in 1991/92, has been in surplus in each of the
three years, though Haryana and Karnataka had relatively small deficits in the first year
and surpluses in the other two. In absolute terms, Maharashtra's deficits have been large
but expressed as percentages of total receipts they have not risen above 5.3 percent. The
situation in Kerala and, particularly, in Tamil Nadu is worse. In the former the deficits
have been between 12. 1 and 14.4 percent of receipts while in the latter the range has
been from 15.1 to 28.1 percent. Compared to the 'all-states' position on the revenue
account Assam, Haryana, Karnataka and Maharashtra are more healthy and Kerala and
Tamil Nadu less healthy. The seriousness of Assam's position, however, with its huge
dependence on grants indicated above, is underestimated by these figures.
17.   All states have a gross fiscal deficit. As a percentage of receipts these were
estimated in the 1993/94 budgets at 13.6 in Assam, 15.6 in Haryana, 20.5 in Karnataka,
20.9 in Maharashtra, 26.0 in Kerala and 28.9 percent in Tamil Nadu. For 'all states'
the percentage was 23.7. The overall surplus/deficit position provides a slightly different
picture. This measure differs from the gross fiscal deficit in that it demonstrates the
situation after accounting for net loans from the center. As a percentage of receipts the
overall deficits are highest in Assam (9.3) and Tamil Nadu (4.6). In none of the other
IDA DPEP states was the deficit higher than 1.5 percent of receipts.



- 249 -                            Annex 15
Page 5

18.   In principle borrowing is not necessarily a cause for concern. When a significant
proportion of the borrowing requirement (gross fiscal deficit) is for financing the revenue
account, however, this places a severe constraint on capital outlays. Assam and Haryana
apparently have surplus' on the revenue account and low net borrowing requirements.
Karnataka again has a surplus but borrows significantly for capital requirements.
Maharashtra has a relatively modest deficit and borrows mainly to finance capital
requirements. While Kerala is a moderate borrower, a relatively high proportion of this
is to fund the revenue deficit. The state in the most extreme position (not only with
regard to the six described here but among all states) is Tamil Nadu which has a high
overall borrowing requirement of which over 60 percent is required to cover the revenue
deficit. Such a situation is likely to be unsustainable.
Education Finance
19.   Expenditures. In table 2, revenue expenditures made by departments of education
and by all other departments on education and training are expressed in terms of their
shares in state domestic product (SDP), and total government expenditure and per capita
allocations for 1991/92. State per capita incomes are also presented. Apart from
Haryana, each of the six DPEP states was spending a higher proportion of state domestic
product on education than the average state in India. Kerala stands out as the state



- 250 -                          Annex 15
Page 6
devoting the highest share to education (6.5 percent), followed by Assam (5.9 percent)
and Tamil Nadu (5.8 percent). Shares in the other three states were between 3.1 and 4.1
percent.
20.   The pattern for the share of education in state government total expenditure is
similar to that for SDP. Kerala devotes the highest percentage, averaging around 25
percent followed by Assam and Tamil Nadu. The share in Karnataka and Haryana is
currently around one third below that in Kerala. Comparing 1985-86 with 1992-93, very
few changes have occurred to shares within states.
Table 2. Education Expenditures Across Six States. 1991-92
Measure        Assam   Haryana   Karnataka    Kerala    Maharashtra    Tamil Nadu
As % SDP         5.9      3.1        4.1        6.5         4.0           5.8
As % total      23.6      18.4      19.9        25.2        22.4          22.0
budget

expenditure

SDP
Source: Tables supplied by Ministry of Human Resource Development.
21.   The very large differences in education's share of total expenditure and GDP do
not result in commensurate differences in educational expenditure per capita; all states
spend 200-280 Rs per capita on education. While per capita expenditures are highest in
Kerala, the lowest (in Karnataka) is 24 percent below. A greater "effort" is required in
Kerala, Assam and Tamil Nadu to reach the same level of provision as in the three more
wealthy states. The ability of these relatively poorer states to continue to provide this
effort would depend upon the continued receipt of central government grants in Assam
and high levels of borrowing in Tamil Nadu to fund the deficit on the revenue account.
22.   The most recent year for which data are available on actual expenditures by level
of education by state is for 1990/91. The states spend between 46 and 56 percent of
expenditure on elementary education and between 27 and 35 percent on secondary
schooling. A comparison of expenditure data for 1989/90 with the budgeted estimates
for 1992/93, projects a reduced share for higher education (all states but Kerala).
23.    Non-plan expenditures account for the largest share of state education
expenditures (89.5 percent in 1990/91). For the education departments of the IDA DPEP
states, the non-plan expenditure shares were:
Assam   1.6    Haryana       93.1    Karnataka    89.6
Kerala  97.0    Maharashtra  97.5    Tamil Nadu   95.0



- 251 -                          Annex 15
Page 7
For other departments that offer "educational" services-such as school incentive
schemes for "backward" groups-the share of non-plan expenditures is lower.
24.   Of total pLan expenditure on education, 40 percent comes from departments other
than the department of education. Table 3 shows the proportion of plan expenditures on
primary education as a share of total primary education expenditures for the six states.
Table 3. Plan Expenditures on Primary Education as Share of Total Pnmaiy
Education Expenditures. 1990-91 to 1992-93. Six States
(percent)
State                       1990-91        1991-92         1992-93
Assam                        35.3            45.5           43.1
Haryana                       8.1            9.6            13.9
Kamataka                      8.2            13.6           23.6
Kerala                        0.4            0.4             0.7
Maharashtra                   1.4            3.6             3.4
Tamil Nadu                    6.6            4.8             5.0
Source: Ministry of Human Resource Development: Analysis of Budgeted Expenditure on Education
1990/91 to 1992/93.
25.   In Assam, plan expenditures are a very high proportion of the total, but is not a
consequence of large expansionary programs. Rather it reflects the paucity of non plan
revenues and the necessity to use plan grants to finance the recurrent costs. This must
be regarded as a very worrying situation.
26.   In Karnataka, there is a very clear upward trend in plan proportions with the
current level being almost a quarter of total expenditures; and a similar trend can be seen
for Haryana over the past three years, though at a much lower rate.
27.   The very low plan expenditures in Kerala imply considerable financial constraints
on new primary education programs. The same is true, though to a lesser extent, in
Maharashtra and Tamil Nadu. It needs reiterating that on average across all states and
levels of education, 40 percent of plan expenditures on education are the responsibility
of departments other than education and that much of these fund incentive programs.
28.   Centrally sponsored schemes. In 1991/92 across all states, centrally sponsored
scheme (CSS) allocations were equal to 34 percent of total state plan expenditures on
primary education. Most CSS support formal and non-formal elementary education and,
apart from grants for non-formal education for boys, none requires matching
expenditures.
29.   On the whole, the six IDA/DPEP states have received below average CSS
allocations. For Assam the proportion is relatively small-mainly because of abnormally
high plan allocations-and are likely to increase as a proportion of funding for new



- 252 -
Annex 15
Page 8
activities. In Haryana, the grants have been relatively small, amounting to around a fifth
of state plan expenditure in 1991/92. Although CSS grants appear to have increased
rapidly in Karnataka in 1991/92 to a level of almost 30 percent of state funding, they
generally have been equivalent to around 10 to 15 percent. In Kerala the grants are
small, but state plan expenditures are even smaller. The pattern in Maharashtra is
similar to that in Karnataka. In 1991/92 there appears to have been a major surge in
grants while on average they have been equal to around 20 percent of state expenditures.
Finally, the ratio seems to be more significant in Tamil Nadu varying between one
quarter and one half.  Because state plan expenditures incorporate those made by
departments other than education, the values of central government expenditures relative
to state plan expenditures on new educational activities are actually significantly higher
than those implied above.
B. DPEP FINANCIAL GUIDELINES
30.   Education finance patterns have implications for four financial aspects of the
DPEP Guidelines: the requirement for a 15 percent contribution from the state, project
additionality, financial sustainability and the anticipated expansion of the program.
State contribution
31.   The requirement for states to fund 15 percent of the total project cost should not
cause problems. The annual average allocations likely to be required from the states
over the project period (assuming a seven year project) are shown below together with
the 1992/93 actual plan expenditures and 1993/94 outlays on elementary education across
the six states: (Rs crores).
Assam      Haryana    Karnataka    Kerala    Maharashtra    Tamil N.
1992/93      72.0        10.2       118.8        2.4        38.8         27.5
1993/94     102.7        19.6       202.1        3.2        41.5         38.3
1994-2000     3.7         3.4         3.9        2.3         4.4          2.5
32.   The envisaged annual expenditures over the project period are equal to 3.6, 17.3,
1.9, 71.9, 10.6 and 6.5 percent of 1993/94 plan outlays on elementary education across
the six states (taken alphabetically). In the state where the required allocation will be
equal to over two thirds of the existing outlay (Kerala), the absolute level of resources
involved is the lowest-and equal to just 0.6 percent of the non plan expenditures on
elementary education. Probably the state with the largest potential problem is Assam
where the low percentage hides a situation where virtually all plan expenditures are
necessary for funding non plan activities. The Planning Commission has agreed to the
incorporation of, at least, the required level of resources for DPEP in each of the
1994/95 annual state plans.



- 253 -                          Annex 15
Page 9
Additionality
33.   The requirement that the state's contribution to DPEP is a net addition to plan
expenditures on elementary education is central to GOIs intention that the program leads
to overall increases in resources for education. Additionality is feasible for all states,
although state budgets will require careful monitoring. Only in Tamil Nadu were real
expenditures in both elementary and general education not maintained between 1990/91
and 1992/93.
34.   The concept of additionality adopted by the Government reflects the one agreed
between IDA and the Government of India for the Social Safety Net arrangements. That
is, plan expenditures, minus the states' DPEP contributions, should be at levels that at
least maintain real resources for the education sector in general and for elementary
education in particular at their 1991/92 levels. On the basis of recent evidence this
requirement should not be too difficult to achieve for most of the states but will require
careful monitoring in others. Plan expenditures at constant prices between 1990/91 and
1992/93 are described in Table 4.
Table 4. Plan Expenditures on Elementara and General Education 1990/91 to 1992/93
in IDA DPEP States
(Constant 1990/91 Rs crores)

State    1990/91      1991/92      1992/93      1990/91      1991/92     1992/93
Assam     43.0        48.4          72.0          79.1         90.9        138.2
Haryana    7.4         8.5          10.2          24.6         32.3        37.2
Kamataka  33.3        51.0         118.8          79.0         82.6        180.5
Kerala     1.7         1.3          2.4           9.6          9.3         12.9
Maharashtra 6.1       17.9          38.8          20.6         64.9        62.0
Tanii Nadu 34.8      31.5          27.5          91.0         79.3        70.4
35.  In Tamil Nadu real expenditures were lower in 1991/92 than in 1990/91 on both
elementary education and general education.  In Maharashtra, general education
expenditures were slightly lower in 1992/93 than in the previous year though elementary
expenditures were higher. While elementary and general education expenditures fell in
Kerala in 1991/92, they fully recovered in the following year. In the other states, real
expenditures on both elementary and general education increased each year. However,
in Assam, the large increases do not reflect new programs but rather the necessity for
ongoing programs to be funded through plan expenditures as a result of severe difficulties



- 254 -                           Annex 15
Page 10
in the non plan budget. The significant increases in plan elementary expenditures in
Karnataka and Maharashtra partly reflect substantial expansion of their incentive
programs. Expenditures in Haryana, while relatively low, have maintained an upward
trend.
Sustainability
36.   The ability of a state to sustain post project recurrent costs on the non plan budget
depends on a number of factors. Among these are (a) the structure of the project itself
and the consequent level of recurring expenditures, (b) the outcome of the Tenth Finance
Commission, in the second half of 1994, which will set the pattern for non discretionary
central transfers over the coming years, (c) the future level and pattem of discretionary
transfers, (d) the strength of the economy and increases in state revenues and (e) the
willingness and ability of govemments to reorient their activities and restructure their
budgets. The uncertainties of (b)-(e) are so great that only the past and present can be
used as a guide to the future. However, since it is the declared intention of those
participating in DPEP to accelerate the development of primary education, partly through
an increase in the level of resources, the guide must be regarded as partial.
37.   During the project life, the composition and scale of activities implemented is
likely to change from that currently proposed. However, using the existing programs and
assuming a real rate of expenditure growth for elementary education of 4.0 percent a
year over the full project life across each state, the data in table 5 indicate that the annual
recurring costs would roughly be equivalent to between one third and twice the normal
increase in non plan expenditures allocated to elementary education in the year following
the end of the seven year project. For each of the three southern states plus Maharashtra
the required increase would be below 50 percent of that "normally" allocated (although
since these states each has substantial incentive programs the full costs of increased
enrollments would be additional).
Table 5. Projections of Non Plan Expenditures on Elementara Education in IDA
DPEP States
(Constant 1993/94 Rs crores)

State     plan outlay  (4 percent p. a.)  after project   expenditures  percent

- 255 -                          Annex 15
Page 11
38.   The increases would average around Rs12 crores compared to an average non plan
budget of around Rs850 crores. In Assam the requirement is a little higher and the
finalization of the work program could result in substantially higher recurring costs than
those reflected in the submissions and recorded in table 5. If the final program structure
reflects those of the other five states, the non plan increase required to sustain it could
be equal to the "normal" allocation. The state which appear to be most out of line is
Haryana where the currently low non plan allocations will result in the recurring costs
of the project being twice the level of "normal" non plan increases. While the state's
proposals are less recurrent cost intensive than in either Maharashtra or Karnataka, the
current low levels of non plan expenditures result in their budgetary impact being much
greater. Haryana has the third highest per capita income across the major states. The
required increases in the non plan budget will be within the government's means if the
political will exists.
39.   Another way of viewing the requirements is to compare it with a "normal" 4
percent increase in the non plan budget. In the year following the end of the project, the
requirement for sustainability would be an increase of 6.4 percent in Assam, 12.0 percent
in Haryana, 5.9 percent in Karnataka, 5.4 percent in Kerala, 5.6 percent in Maharashtra
and 5.6 percent in Tamil Nadu.
40.   A third way is to consider the annual rate of growth required over the whole
project period which would fully finance the recurring cost of project items at the end
of the project. This would take into account salaries, which are a significant part of all
states' programs and the costs of which will need to be picked up by the states over the
life of the project. Instead of the 4.0 percent adopted, the growth rates would need to
be 4.3 percent in Assam, 5.0 percent in Haryana, and 4.2 percent in Karnataka, Kerala,
Maharashtra and Tamil Nadu.
41.   In both these sets of calculation the reservation regarding the validity of the
recurrent cost estimates in the Assam submission needs to be emphasized. In addition,
referring back to the previous analysis of state finances the overall budgetary situation
in both Assam and Tamil Nadu appears problematic.
42.   The considerations above imply that a program to accelerate the quantity and
quality of primary education in a small number of districts will necessitate increasing the
share of the primary education budget but not beyond that possible. This assumes that
(a) the overall financial situations in Assam and Tamil Nadu are restructured in ways that
more closely equate non plan resources with the demands on the budget and (b) a
political will to increase the priority to elementary education is gradually developed in
Haryana.
Expansion of DPEP
43.   The financial strength of the IDA/DPEP states varies considerably. Assam,
Kerala and Tamil Nadu are the weakest, though each demonstrates this in different ways.



256 -                          Annex 15
Page 12
The first two are able to fund very few plan activities while the latter has the highest
borrowing requirement across the states. Maharashtra, Karnataka and Haryana are
relatively strong. All states, however, are likely to be adversely affected by the central
government's stated intention to pursue policies of fiscal correction which will imply
restraints on both grants and lending to states.
44.   The DPEP program is in its first phase within the six states. Over the next
decade or so the number of districts covered will be expanded and the required levels of
both plan and non plan expenditures to fund and sustain the investments, increased
enrollments and new programs will substantially increase. This is desirable. The issue
is where the resources will come from. Among the alternatives are:
(a)   increased state revenues.  This possibility will vary between states.
Already Tamil Nadu and Kerala have the highest levels of tax per capita
and the possibilities of increased non tax revenues including higher
payments for services will need to be considered. Other states such as
Haryana have much lower tax/income ratios.
(b)   reductions in net expenditures in other sectors. Given the relatively low
levels of spending on education in India this approach should be
encouraged.
(c)   restructuring expenditures within the education sector. While it is not
impossible to increase further the share for primary education, the share
for higher education is already relatively low and has been decreasing over
the past decade.
(d)   within primary education there may be possibilities to reduce incremental
capital cost through double shift systems, new technologies and perhaps
fewer but larger schools. These are unlikely to reduce simultaneously the
per pupil recurrent costs of teachers and materials and if they did there
would almost certainly be a reduction in quality. One possible area to find
savings is among the several incentive programs. There is a general but
unproven belief that these are effective in increasing enrollments and
retention. Given their size (in Tamil Nadu they are equivalent to an
increase in unit costs of over 20 percent across all children), however, and
the evidence from both Karnataka and Maharashtra that the schemes are
being widened, their effectiveness needs to be assessed.
State Specific Analyses
45.   Assam. Compared to other states, plan expenditure on elementary education is
large relative to non plan being around a quarter of total expenditure in 1993/4.
Expenditures in the two years 1991/2 and 1992/3 were Rs55 crores and Rs91 crores and



- 257 -                         Annex 15
Page 13
are anticipated to be Rs103 crores in 1993/94. As a result of the serious financial
problems of the state, however, the plan expenditures tend not to be used for financing
new activities as is usual but rather for financing activities begun during the Sixth and
Seventh Plan periods. Normally these activities would by now be financed by non plan
expenditures but revenues are insufficient. The deficit on the revenue account was
RsIO.3 crores in 1991/92, Rs23.8 crores in 1992/93 and is estimated to be Rs 91.0
crores in 1993/94. Part of the deficit results from large repayments on past loans. The
numbers of primary schools and teachers have been stationary since 1991/92. Since 1991
Assam has been categorized as Special Category State which results in central plan
assistance being transferred on terms of 90 percent grant and 10 percent loan as opposed
to the normal terms of 70 percent loan and 30 percent grant. This change should lead
to a reduction in liabilities from 1994/95 onwards and the beginning of a more healthy
financial situation.
46.   Haryana.  Elementary education expenditures in the Seventh Plan were 2.5
percent of the total. In the Eighth Plan the allocation is equal to 2.4 percent although
in the first year, 1992/93, actual expenditures were equal to only 1.7 percent and the
allocation for 1993/94 is below the average annual required to achieve the Eighth Plan
total. In absolute terms, actual plan expenditures on elementary education increased from
Rs7.3 to Rs9.9 to RsI2.7 crores over the past three years and Rsl9.6 crores were
budgeted for 1993/94. Of some concern, however, is that expenditures in 1992/3 were
22 percent less than those budgeted. Haryana's financial position is one of the healthiest
in the country and it is reported to be state policy that, in the event of reductions in plan
allocations, the three areas totally safeguarded are centrally sponsored schemes,
externally aided projects and the Electricity Board. While there is an undoubted ability
to ensure that plan allocations to elementary education in addition to those required for
DPEP will continue to grow, the comparatively poor record in the past and the
experience (common to most states) of reductions in budgeted outlays will require careful
monitoring.
47.   Karnataka.  The overall financial situation of Karnataka state is one of the
strongest in the country. Since 1989/90 deficits on the revenue account have declined
continuously and in 1992/3 were equal to less than one percent of expenditures. A
considerable surplus is projected for the current year. Over the period 1985/6 to 1992/3,
expenditures on primary education increased at a faster rate than overall state
expenditures. Plan expenditures on elementary education reported in the state proposal
have increased substantially over the past four years from Rs33.3 crores in 1990/1 to
Rs147.4 in 1992/3 and a budgeted Rs202.1 crores in 1993/4. Much of this increase in
the past two years is due to an extension of various incentive programs. Plan allocations
to the education sector as a whole have increased from 3.1 percent of the total in the
Seventh Plan to 8.2, 12.4, 23.6 and 26.8 percent in each of the last four years. At the
same time, the share of primary education within general education has also increased.
48.   Kerala. Of the six states Kerala has the second highest projected revenue account
deficit for 1993/4. Partly as a result its plan expenditures are relatively low. Currently



- 258 -                         Annex 15
Page 14
it spends a higher proportion of its domestic product and total budget expenditure on
education than does any other state. In 1992/3 education consumed 34 percent of non
plan revenue expenditures and 11 percent of plan revenue expenditures. Looked at in
another way, however, plan expenditures on primary education are a tiny part of total
primary expenditures-less that one percent in each of the last four years (1990/1 -
1993/4).  In absolute terms, they were Rsl.7, Rsl.5, Rs3.0 and Rs3.2 crores
respectively. Allocations from centrally sponsored schemes (eg operation blackboard)
have tended to exceed those made by the state.
49.   Maharashtra. Maharashtra is one of the few revenue surplus states with relatively
limited amounts of borrowing required, and only for capital expenditures.  Plan
expenditures on elementary education increased from Rs6.0 to Rs2O.3 to Rs48.2 crores
over the period 1990/1 to 1992/3. What is then of concern is that the outlay for 1993/4
fell to Rs41.5 crores. In addition, while the overall size of the Eighth Plan outlay does
not imply much annual growth in real terms compared to 1992/3 the component for
incentives will have to grow as enrollments increase so leaving little room for new
directly educational activities.
50.   Tamil Nadu. Tamil Nadu has incurred large deficits on its revenue account over
recent years. In 1992/3 the deficit was equal to 24 percent of the combined deficit across
all states. It is the only state in which this deficit is equal to over 60 percent of its gross
fiscal deficit (borrowing requirement). Expenditure on education is a relatively high
proportion of domestic product and total state expenditure. The government operates the
most comprehensive set of incentives programs across the states. The combined cost of
books, uniforms and footwear schemes in 1990/1 was equal to 55 percent of elementary
plan expenditure while the cost of the school meals program was over twice as large.
Education as a whole is projected to receive a smaller share of total plan expenditures
during the Eighth Plan than in the Seventh.

Supervision Objectives and Strategy
1.       Bank supervision for DPEP would be integrated with supervision for the UP Basic
Education Project (Credit 2509-IN) and managed from the New Delhi Resident Mission. The
objectives of the combined supervision programl for the two credits would be to:
a.       assist in the development of the capacity of the DPEP BureaLu and state
implementation societies to supervise and evaluate the GOI's primary
education development program, including both DPEP investments and
earlier state-specific investment projects;
b.       ensure timely and effective imlplemiientation of agreed action programs and
disbursements;
c.       facilitate collaboration among donors supporting primary education
development in India.
2.       Capacity development would be an essential elemenit of IDA supervision. The size
of India's primary education investmnent program, andl the wide geographical distribution of a
multitude of highly decentralized activities, would inake it difficult for IDA to provide effective
implementation support in the traditional headquarters ImlissiOI mode. To assist with capacity
development, IDA staff based in the Resident Mission would, on a continuouls basis:
a.       develop a cadre of national consultants, both individuals and firms, to
assist IDA staff in capacity development and supervision activities;
b.       provide both formal and on-the-job training to DPEP staff in program
supervision techlniques;
c.       together with DPEP nationial staff, provide the samie training to the staff
of state implemenitationi societies.
3.       In parallel, and with support from  the Resident Mission's ProcLuremnent and
Disbursement Unit, IDA staff would monitor implementation, procurement and disburseinent on
a quarterly basis through missions to states and districts, and through close cooperation witlh the
DPEP Bureau and the Department of Economic Affairs.
4.       DPEP would be supported by the European Commilunity and IDA; additional financing
from ODA and other bi-lateral donors is anticipated. Supervision woulcl be carried out in close



Annex 16
- 260 -                                  Page 2
coordination with all program  donors. Methods would inclUde regular quarterly mieetinigs of
donors with DPEP managemienit, joint suL)ervision mlissions at least once a year, and fLull
exchange of supervision reports prepared by DPEP andl the various donors.
5.        Continuious IDA support tor imiplemilenitationi wOUld be structuired in Suchi a way as to
bring appropriate techilical expertise to bear on implementation issues as they miighit arise.
While most of the supervision support would be provided by IDA staff and national consuIltants,
the expertise of senior Bank staff and consuIltanits would be engaged as needed.

INDIA: District Primary Education Program
Monitoring Report Format
1.    All DPEP sub-projects (national, state and district) would report on progress
every three months. Receipt of completed reports by the DPEP Bureau would be a
condition of advance of funds. The basic schedule for reporting and advances would
be:
June        -      First advance of 50 percent of approved annual
expenditures
September   -     Submission of Quarterly Progress Report
November   -       Submission of Quarterly Progress Report
December   -      Advance of second annual fund release, amount
determined by progress
March       -      Submission of Quarterly Progress Report
May         -      Submission of Quarterly Progress Report
2.    In addition to the quarterly Progress Reports, a summary annual report would
be prepared in December for the previous calendar year's activities.
3.    The initial format to be used for the quarterly Progress Report is attached.
This would be modified periodically as need.
